# --------------------------------------------------------
# Swin Transformer

import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F
from itertools import repeat
import collections.abc

class Mlp(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, in_features, hidden_features=None, out_features=None, act_layer=nn.GELU, drop=0.):
        super().__init__()
        out_features = out_features or in_features
        hidden_features = hidden_features or in_features
        self.fc1 = nn.Linear(in_features, hidden_features)
        self.act = act_layer()
        self.fc2 = nn.Linear(hidden_features, out_features)
        self.drop = nn.Dropout(drop)

    def forward(self, x):
        x = self.fc1(x)
        x = self.act(x)
        x = self.drop(x)
        x = self.fc2(x)
        x = self.drop(x)
        return x


def window_partition(x, window_size):
    """
    Args:
        x: (B, H, W, C)
        window_size (int): window size

    Returns:
        windows: (num_windows*B, window_size, window_size, C)
    """
    B, H, W, C = x.shape
    x = x.view(B, H // window_size, window_size, W // window_size, window_size, C)
    windows = x.permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5).contiguous().view(-1, window_size, window_size, C)
    return windows


def window_reverse(windows, window_size, H, W):
    """
    Args:
        windows: (num_windows*B, window_size, window_size, C)
        window_size (int): Window size
        H (int): Height of image
        W (int): Width of image

    Returns:
        x: (B, H, W, C)
    """
    B = int(windows.shape[0] / (H * W / window_size / window_size))
    x = windows.view(B, H // window_size, W // window_size, window_size, window_size, -1)
    x = x.permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5).contiguous().view(B, H, W, -1)
    return x


class SwinMLPBlock(nn.Module):
    r""" Swin MLP Block.

    Args:
        dim (int): Number of input channels.
        input_resolution (tuple[int]): Input resulotion.
        num_heads (int): Number of attention heads.
        window_size (int): Window size.
        shift_size (int): Shift size for SW-MSA.
        mlp_ratio (float): Ratio of mlp hidden dim to embedding dim.
        drop (float, optional): Dropout rate. Default: 0.0
        drop_path (float, optional): Stochastic depth rate. Default: 0.0
        act_layer (nn.Module, optional): Activation layer. Default: nn.GELU
        norm_layer (nn.Module, optional): Normalization layer.  Default: nn.LayerNorm
    """

    def __init__(self, dim, input_resolution, num_heads, window_size=7, shift_size=0,
                 mlp_ratio=4., drop=0., drop_path=0.,
                 act_layer=nn.GELU, norm_layer=nn.LayerNorm):
        super().__init__()
        self.dim = dim
        self.input_resolution = input_resolution
        self.num_heads = num_heads
        self.window_size = window_size
        self.shift_size = shift_size
        self.mlp_ratio = mlp_ratio
        if min(self.input_resolution) <= self.window_size:
            # if window size is larger than input resolution, we don't partition windows
            self.shift_size = 0
            self.window_size = min(self.input_resolution)
        assert 0 <= self.shift_size < self.window_size, "shift_size must in 0-window_size"

        self.padding = [self.window_size - self.shift_size, self.shift_size,
                        self.window_size - self.shift_size, self.shift_size]  # P_l,P_r,P_t,P_b

        self.norm1 = norm_layer(dim)
        # use group convolution to implement multi-head MLP
        self.spatial_mlp = nn.Conv1d(self.num_heads * self.window_size ** 2,
                                     self.num_heads * self.window_size ** 2,
                                     kernel_size=1,
                                     groups=self.num_heads)

        self.drop_path = nn.Identity()
        self.norm2 = norm_layer(dim)
        mlp_hidden_dim = int(dim * mlp_ratio)
        self.mlp = Mlp(in_features=dim, hidden_features=mlp_hidden_dim, act_layer=act_layer, drop=drop)

    def forward(self, x):
        H, W = self.input_resolution
        B, L, C = x.shape
        assert L == H * W, "input feature has wrong size"

        shortcut = x
        x = self.norm1(x)
        x = x.view(B, H, W, C)

        # shift
        if self.shift_size > 0:
            P_l, P_r, P_t, P_b = self.padding
            shifted_x = F.pad(x, [0, 0, P_l, P_r, P_t, P_b], "constant", 0)
        else:
            shifted_x = x
        _, _H, _W, _ = shifted_x.shape

        # partition windows
        x_windows = window_partition(shifted_x, self.window_size)  # nW*B, window_size, window_size, C
        x_windows = x_windows.view(-1, self.window_size * self.window_size, C)  # nW*B, window_size*window_size, C

        # Window/Shifted-Window Spatial MLP
        x_windows_heads = x_windows.view(-1, self.window_size * self.window_size, self.num_heads, C // self.num_heads)
        x_windows_heads = x_windows_heads.transpose(1, 2)  # nW*B, nH, window_size*window_size, C//nH
        x_windows_heads = x_windows_heads.reshape(-1, self.num_heads * self.window_size * self.window_size,
                                                  C // self.num_heads)
        spatial_mlp_windows = self.spatial_mlp(x_windows_heads)  # nW*B, nH*window_size*window_size, C//nH
        spatial_mlp_windows = spatial_mlp_windows.view(-1, self.num_heads, self.window_size * self.window_size,
                                                       C // self.num_heads).transpose(1, 2)
        spatial_mlp_windows = spatial_mlp_windows.reshape(-1, self.window_size * self.window_size, C)

        # merge windows
        spatial_mlp_windows = spatial_mlp_windows.reshape(-1, self.window_size, self.window_size, C)
        shifted_x = window_reverse(spatial_mlp_windows, self.window_size, _H, _W)  # B H' W' C

        # reverse shift
        if self.shift_size > 0:
            P_l, P_r, P_t, P_b = self.padding
            x = shifted_x[:, P_t:-P_b, P_l:-P_r, :].contiguous()
        else:
            x = shifted_x
        x = x.view(B, H * W, C)

        # FFN
        x = shortcut + self.drop_path(x)
        x = x + self.drop_path(self.mlp(self.norm2(x)))

        return x


class PatchMerging(nn.Module):
    r""" Patch Merging Layer.

    Args:
        input_resolution (tuple[int]): Resolution of input feature.
        dim (int): Number of input channels.
        norm_layer (nn.Module, optional): Normalization layer.  Default: nn.LayerNorm
    """

    def __init__(self, input_resolution, dim, norm_layer=nn.LayerNorm):
        super().__init__()
        self.input_resolution = input_resolution
        self.dim = dim
        self.reduction = nn.Linear(4 * dim, 2 * dim, bias=False)
        self.norm = norm_layer(4 * dim)

    def forward(self, x):
        """
        x: B, H*W, C
        """
        H, W = self.input_resolution
        B, L, C = x.shape
        assert L == H * W, "input feature has wrong size"
        assert H % 2 == 0 and W % 2 == 0, f"x size ({H}*{W}) are not even."

        x = x.view(B, H, W, C)

        x0 = x[:, 0::2, 0::2, :]  # B H/2 W/2 C
        x1 = x[:, 1::2, 0::2, :]  # B H/2 W/2 C
        x2 = x[:, 0::2, 1::2, :]  # B H/2 W/2 C
        x3 = x[:, 1::2, 1::2, :]  # B H/2 W/2 C
        x = torch.cat([x0, x1, x2, x3], -1)  # B H/2 W/2 4*C
        x = x.view(B, -1, 4 * C)  # B H/2*W/2 4*C

        x = self.norm(x)
        x = self.reduction(x)

        return x

class BasicLayer(nn.Module):
    """ A basic Swin MLP layer for one stage.

    Args:
        dim (int): Number of input channels.
        input_resolution (tuple[int]): Input resolution.
        depth (int): Number of blocks.
        num_heads (int): Number of attention heads.
        window_size (int): Local window size.
        mlp_ratio (float): Ratio of mlp hidden dim to embedding dim.
        drop (float, optional): Dropout rate. Default: 0.0
        drop_path (float | tuple[float], optional): Stochastic depth rate. Default: 0.0
        norm_layer (nn.Module, optional): Normalization layer. Default: nn.LayerNorm
        downsample (nn.Module | None, optional): Downsample layer at the end of the layer. Default: None
        use_checkpoint (bool): Whether to use checkpointing to save memory. Default: False.
    """

    def __init__(self, dim, input_resolution, depth, num_heads, window_size,
                 mlp_ratio=4., drop=0., drop_path=0.,
                 norm_layer=nn.LayerNorm, downsample=None, use_checkpoint=False):

        super().__init__()
        self.dim = dim
        self.input_resolution = input_resolution
        self.depth = depth
        self.use_checkpoint = use_checkpoint

        # build blocks
        self.blocks = nn.ModuleList([
            SwinMLPBlock(dim=dim, input_resolution=input_resolution,
                         num_heads=num_heads, window_size=window_size,
                         shift_size=0 if (i % 2 == 0) else window_size // 2,
                         mlp_ratio=mlp_ratio,
                         drop=drop,
                         drop_path=drop_path[i] if isinstance(drop_path, list) else drop_path,
                         norm_layer=norm_layer)
            for i in range(depth)])

        # patch merging layer
        if downsample is not None:
            self.downsample = downsample(input_resolution, dim=dim, norm_layer=norm_layer)
        else:
            self.downsample = None

    def forward(self, x):
        for blk in self.blocks:
            if self.use_checkpoint:
                x = checkpoint.checkpoint(blk, x)
            else:
                x = blk(x)
        if self.downsample is not None:
            x = self.downsample(x)
        return x

def _ntuple(n):
    def parse(x):
        if isinstance(x, collections.abc.Iterable) and not isinstance(x, str):
            return tuple(x)
        return tuple(repeat(x, n))
    return parse
to_2tuple = _ntuple(2)

class PatchEmbed(nn.Module):
    r""" Image to Patch Embedding

    Args:
        img_size (int): Image size.  Default: 224.
        patch_size (int): Patch token size. Default: 4.
        in_chans (int): Number of input image channels. Default: 3.
        embed_dim (int): Number of linear projection output channels. Default: 96.
        norm_layer (nn.Module, optional): Normalization layer. Default: None
    """

    def __init__(self, img_size=224, patch_size=4, in_chans=3, embed_dim=96, norm_layer=None):
        super().__init__()
        img_size = to_2tuple(img_size)
        patch_size = to_2tuple(patch_size)
        patches_resolution = [img_size[0] // patch_size[0], img_size[1] // patch_size[1]]
        self.img_size = img_size
        self.patch_size = patch_size
        self.patches_resolution = patches_resolution
        self.num_patches = patches_resolution[0] * patches_resolution[1]

        self.in_chans = in_chans
        self.embed_dim = embed_dim

        self.proj = nn.Conv2d(in_chans, embed_dim, kernel_size=patch_size, stride=patch_size)
        if norm_layer is not None:
            self.norm = norm_layer(embed_dim)
        else:
            self.norm = None

    def forward(self, x):
        B, C, H, W = x.shape
        # FIXME look at relaxing size constraints
        assert H == self.img_size[0] and W == self.img_size[1], \
            f"Input image size ({H}*{W}) doesn't match model ({self.img_size[0]}*{self.img_size[1]})."
        x = self.proj(x).flatten(2).transpose(1, 2)  # B Ph*Pw C
        if self.norm is not None:
            x = self.norm(x)
        return x

    def flops(self):
        Ho, Wo = self.patches_resolution
        flops = Ho * Wo * self.embed_dim * self.in_chans * (self.patch_size[0] * self.patch_size[1])
        if self.norm is not None:
            flops += Ho * Wo * self.embed_dim
        return flops


class Model(nn.Module):
    r""" Swin MLP

    Args:
        img_size (int | tuple(int)): Input image size. Default 224
        patch_size (int | tuple(int)): Patch size. Default: 4
        in_chans (int): Number of input image channels. Default: 3
        num_classes (int): Number of classes for classification head. Default: 1000
        embed_dim (int): Patch embedding dimension. Default: 96
        depths (tuple(int)): Depth of each Swin MLP layer.
        num_heads (tuple(int)): Number of attention heads in different layers.
        window_size (int): Window size. Default: 7
        mlp_ratio (float): Ratio of mlp hidden dim to embedding dim. Default: 4
        drop_rate (float): Dropout rate. Default: 0
        drop_path_rate (float): Stochastic depth rate. Default: 0.1
        norm_layer (nn.Module): Normalization layer. Default: nn.LayerNorm.
        patch_norm (bool): If True, add normalization after patch embedding. Default: True
        use_checkpoint (bool): Whether to use checkpointing to save memory. Default: False
    """

    def __init__(self, img_size=224, patch_size=4, in_chans=3, num_classes=1000,
                 embed_dim=96, depths=[2, 2, 6, 2], num_heads=[3, 6, 12, 24],
                 window_size=7, mlp_ratio=4., drop_rate=0., drop_path_rate=0.1,
                 norm_layer=nn.LayerNorm, patch_norm=True,
                 use_checkpoint=False, **kwargs):
        super().__init__()

        self.num_classes = num_classes
        self.num_layers = len(depths)
        self.embed_dim = embed_dim
        self.patch_norm = patch_norm
        self.num_features = int(embed_dim * 2 ** (self.num_layers - 1))
        self.mlp_ratio = mlp_ratio

        # split image into non-overlapping patches
        self.patch_embed = PatchEmbed(
            img_size=img_size, patch_size=patch_size, in_chans=in_chans, embed_dim=embed_dim,
            norm_layer=norm_layer if self.patch_norm else None)
        num_patches = self.patch_embed.num_patches
        patches_resolution = self.patch_embed.patches_resolution
        self.patches_resolution = patches_resolution

        self.pos_drop = nn.Dropout(p=drop_rate)

        # stochastic depth
        dpr = [x.item() for x in torch.linspace(0, drop_path_rate, sum(depths))]  # stochastic depth decay rule

        # build layers
        self.layers = nn.ModuleList()
        for i_layer in range(self.num_layers):
            layer = BasicLayer(dim=int(embed_dim * 2 ** i_layer),
                               input_resolution=(patches_resolution[0] // (2 ** i_layer),
                                                 patches_resolution[1] // (2 ** i_layer)),
                               depth=depths[i_layer],
                               num_heads=num_heads[i_layer],
                               window_size=window_size,
                               mlp_ratio=self.mlp_ratio,
                               drop=drop_rate,
                               drop_path=dpr[sum(depths[:i_layer]):sum(depths[:i_layer + 1])],
                               norm_layer=norm_layer,
                               downsample=PatchMerging if (i_layer < self.num_layers - 1) else None,
                               use_checkpoint=use_checkpoint)
            self.layers.append(layer)

        self.norm = norm_layer(self.num_features)
        self.avgpool = nn.AdaptiveAvgPool1d(1)
        self.head = nn.Linear(self.num_features, num_classes) if num_classes > 0 else nn.Identity()

    def forward_features(self, x):
        x = self.patch_embed(x)
        x = self.pos_drop(x)

        for layer in self.layers:
            x = layer(x)

        x = self.norm(x)  # B L C
        x = self.avgpool(x.transpose(1, 2))  # B C 1
        x = torch.flatten(x, 1)
        return x

    def forward(self, x):
        x = self.forward_features(x)
        x = self.head(x)
        return x
    
batch_size = 10
image_size = 224

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, 3, image_size, image_size)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return []

This is a full Swin MLP model. The goal is to write a custom CUDA kernel that replaces the forward computation. Looking at the model, it has: 1. PatchEmbed: Conv2d + flatten + transpose + optional LayerNorm 2. pos_drop: Dropout 3. Multiple BasicLayer blocks, each containing: - Multiple SwinMLPBlocks - Optional PatchMerging 4. Let me analyze the spatial_mlp block more carefully: ```python x_windows = x_windows.view(-1, self.window_size * self.window_size, C) x_windows_heads = x_windows.view(-1, self.window_size * self.window_size, self.num_heads, C // self.num_heads) x_windows_heads = x_windows_heads.transpose(1, 2) x_windows_heads = x_windows_heads.reshape(-1, self.num_heads * self.window_size * self.window_size, C // self.num_heads) spatial_mlp_windows = self.spatial_mlp(x_windows_heads) spatial_mlp_windows = spatial_mlp_windows.view(-1, self.num_heads, self.window_size * self.window_size, C // self.num_heads).transpose(1, 2) spatial_mlp_windows = spatial_mlp_windows.reshape(-1, self.window_size * self.window_size, C) ``` The `spatial_mlp` is `nn.Conv1d(num_heads * window_size^2, num_heads * window_size^2, kernel_size=1, groups=num_heads)`. A Conv1d with kernel_size=1 is equivalent to a Linear layer. And with groups=num_heads, it's equivalent to num_heads separate Linear layers, each mapping from `window_size^2` to `window_size^2`. The input has shape `(nW*B, num_heads, window_size^2, C//num_heads)`, and the conv is applied independently to each head and each channel slice. This is equivalent to: for each head h and each output channel c', compute a linear combination of the `window_size^2` input values for that head and channel. Wait, let me think about this more carefully. The conv1d input shape is: - batch: nW*B - in_channels: num_heads * window_size^2 - length: C//num_heads The conv groups=num_heads, so each group has `window_size^2` input channels and `window_size^2` output channels, and the kernel size is 1. So for each group g, it's a linear transformation of size `(window_size^2, window_size^2)` applied to the `(window_size^2, C//num_heads)` input for that group. This can be seen as a batch matrix multiplication: For each window position and each channel c in C//num_heads: output[window, head, :, c] = W[head] @ input[window, head, :, c] where W[head] is a `(window_size^2, window_size^2)` matrix. This is just a batched matrix multiplication! And PyTorch's einsum or bmm should be able to do this efficiently. But the reference uses Conv1d. On H100, Conv1d with groups might not be as efficient as a custom kernel or even bmm. Actually, let me reconsider the approach. The most impactful optimization for a transformer-like model on modern GPUs is often to fuse the MLP block (Linear -> GELU -> Linear) with LayerNorm and residual operations. Actually, for conv1d with groups, the operation is: For each batch element b and each group g: For each output channel o in group g and each position c: output[b, g*49 + o, c] = sum_{i=0}^{48} W[g*49 + o, i, 0] * input[b, g*49 + i, c] So it's applying the same linear transform across the "c" dimension (which is C//num_heads = 32). Another thought: the window partition and reverse involve complex memory access patterns but the actual data movement is just reshaping. In PyTorch, these are often implemented as view operations, so they're essentially free (no memory copy) if the tensor is contiguous in the right layout. The `.contiguous()` calls are what cause actual memory copies. In the reference code, after window_partition there's `.contiguous()`, and after window_reverse there's `.contiguous()`. These memory copies are pure overhead. If we write a custom kernel that operates directly on the logical layout, we can avoid these copies. So one strong optimization is: 1. After norm1, x is (B, H, W, C) 2. Instead of window_partition (which involves view + permute + contiguous) then more views, we can write a kernel that reads directly from the (B, H, W, C) layout and computes the spatial_mlp output, then writes to the output (B, H, W, C) layout. This would eliminate several intermediate copies and reshapes. Unflatten, unpartition back to (B, H, W, C) This is a very structured operation. We can write a CUDA kernel that loads a tile of input into shared memory, applies the transform, and writes output. The model is likely memory-bandwidth bound for some operations and latency-bound for others due to small sizes. Key insight: for small batches, the overhead of many small kernel launches is significant. Fusing operations together can dramatically reduce kernel launch overhead and memory bandwidth. So the main optimization should be kernel fusion, especially for the elementwise operations and the MLP blocks. But wait, I said the MLP is compute-bound. Let me reconsider. Actually, PyTorch's bmm supports broadcasting for the batch dimension. So: y_heads = torch.einsum('nhic,hoi->nhoc', x_heads, W) Then add bias if present: y_heads += b.view(1, num_heads, ws*ws, 1) Then reshape back: y = y_heads.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).reshape(-1, ws, ws, C) # Then window reverse + unshift This einsum should be efficient and avoids the Conv1d. But actually, writing a custom CUDA kernel for this einsum could be beneficial because we can control the memory layout better. Writing an efficient matmul for (49, 49) x (49, 32) inside a CUDA kernel is tricky because it's so small. We'd likely just do it with shared memory or even directly in registers. So the spatial_mlp is always applying num_heads separate (49, 49) -> (49, 32) transformations. For each head, the matmul is (49, 49) x (49, 32). The number of heads varies: 3, 6, 12, 24. For stage 4: 24 heads, 10 windows total (B=10, nW=1), so 240 matmuls of (49,49)x(49,32). For stage 1: 3 heads, 640 windows, so 1920 matmuls of (49,49)x(49,32). But realistically, with such small matrices, we won't achieve anywhere near peak utilization. Actually, I think a custom CUDA kernel that loads W into shared memory and processes multiple channels/windows per thread block could be quite effective. Let me think about the kernel design more carefully. For stage 1: - 640 windows, 3 heads, 32 channels per head - Per window+head: transform 49 spatial positions to 49 output spatial positions, for 32 channels Total elements per window+head: 49*32 = 1568 Total windows*heads = 640*3 = 1920 If we process one window+head per thread block: - Block size: maybe 128 or 256 threads - Each thread processes 1568/128 Ōēł 12 output elements - Need to load W (49*49 = 2401 floats Ōēł 9.6KB) into shared memory - Need to load input (49*32 = 1568 floats Ōēł 6.2KB) - Shared memory total: ~16KB, well within limits For 1920 thread blocks, H100 has 132 SMs (I think), so there's enough parallelism. Actually, we can process multiple windows per block or multiple heads per block. But 1920 blocks is already decent parallelism. For stage 4: - 10 windows, 24 heads, 32 channels - windows*heads = 240 Only 240 blocks - that's somewhat low parallelism for H100, but with each block doing some work, it's fine. Now, inside the kernel: 1. Each block handles one (window, head) pair 2. Load W for this head into shared memory (49x49 floats) 3. Load input for this window+head into shared memory (49x32 floats) 4. Compute output = W @ input (matrix multiplication where W is 49x49 and input is 49x32) 5. Write output back We need to be efficient with memory access. Input and output are in the global (B, H, W, C) layout, but we need to access them window by window. For window (b, wx, wy) in the padded image, the channels for head h are at: x[b, wy*ws + jy, wx*ws + jx, h*32 + c] for j in [0, ws*ws), c in [0, 32) This is strided access, but within a window, adjacent spatial positions are (mostly) adjacent in memory if the tensor is (B, H, W, C) contiguous. In a (B, H, W, C) contiguous tensor, x[b, y, x, c] is accessed as x[b*H*W*C + y*W*C + x*C + c]. Within a window at (wy*ws, wx*ws): - j=0 is at (wy*ws, wx*ws) - j=1 is at (wy*ws, wx*ws+1) if jx increments first, or (wy*ws+1, wx*ws) if jy increments first In the reference, window_partition uses: ```python x = x.view(B, H // window_size, window_size, W // window_size, window_size, C) windows = x.permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5).contiguous().view(-1, window_size, window_size, C) ``` This means the access order within a window is: first vary x (the width), then y (the height). So: j = jy*ws + jx Position (wy*ws + jy, wx*ws + jx) In the original tensor, the memory offset is: b*H*W*C + (wy*ws + jy)*W*C + (wx*ws + jx)*C + (h*32 + c) = b*H*W*C + wy*ws*W*C + jy*W*C + wx*ws*C + jx*C + h*32 + c For fixed (b, wy, wx, h, c), as jx varies, we access consecutive memory (stride C). As jy varies, stride is W*C. So if threads in a block read different j values, they won't have coalesced access unless we structure it carefully. Actually, since W*C is typically large, accesses for different jy are far apart. But for a fixed jy, varying jx gives consecutive memory. So if threads read with jx as the fast-varying index, we get coalesced access. In our kernel, if we have 32 threads reading 32 channels at the same spatial position, they'd read: x[b, wy*ws+jy, wx*ws+jx, h*32:(h+1)*32] These are 32 consecutive floats! That's perfectly coalesced. Similarly, if threads read the same channels at different spatial positions... that depends on how we parallelize. For the matmul output[n, h, o, c] = sum_i W[h, o, i] * x[n, h, i, c], the natural parallelization is: - Each thread computes one (o, c) output element - Or each thread computes a range of c for a fixed o - Or each thread block computes all outputs for one (n, h) Given that output is 49*32 = 1568 elements, and a typical block has 128-1024 threads: - With 256 threads: each thread computes ~6 output elements - We can assign threadIdx.x to c (32 channels) and threadIdx.y to o (49 spatial positions) - That gives 32*49 = 1568 threads, which is a lot but manageable - Or we can use 32 threads for c and have each thread loop over o Actually, 1568 threads per block is not ideal for occupancy. A better approach might be: - Have 32 threads (one per channel) - Each thread computes all 49 output spatial positions for its channel - Total threads per block: 32 For each output spatial position o: output[o] = sum_i W[o, i] * input[i] Each thread loops over i=0..48, loads W[o,i] and input[i,c]. Wait, if we have 32 threads where thread c computes: for o in 0..48: sum = 0 for i in 0..48: sum += W_shared[o, i] * input_shared[i, c] output[o, c] = sum This requires W_shared to be 49x49 floats and input_shared to be 49x32 floats. W is loaded once by all threads cooperatively. input is loaded once by all threads cooperatively. Then each thread does 49*49 = 2401 multiply-adds. Total FLOPs per thread: 2401 * 2 Ōēł 4802 FLOPs. With 32 threads per block, each block does 4802*32 Ōēł 154K FLOPs. At 67 TFLOPS per SM, if one block runs on one SM, that's ~2.3 microseconds per block. For 1920 blocks in stage 1, with 132 SMs, total time is roughly 1920/132 * 2.3 Ōēł 33 microseconds (ideal). Actually, this might not be great because 32 threads per block limits occupancy. Each block uses very few warps (just 1 warp). But H100 can run many blocks per SM concurrently. H100 has a limit of 32 blocks per SM. With 1 warp per block, and up to 64 warps per SM (I think H100 is similar to A100 with 64 warps per SM, or maybe more?), we could have up to 32 blocks per SM, giving 32 warps. Now, let me also think about LayerNorm. LayerNorm requires computing mean and variance over C channels. For LayerNorm on (B, L, C): - mean[b,l] = sum_c x[b,l,c] / C - var[b,l] = sum_c (x[b,l,c] - mean)^2 / C - y[b,l,c] = (x[b,l,c] - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) * weight[c] + bias[c] This is a standard operation. PyTorch's LayerNorm is already well-optimized. But if we're writing a custom kernel for the spatial_mlp path, we might want to include LayerNorm in it as well. However, LayerNorm operates on the full (B, L, C) tensor, while the spatial_mlp operates on windows. Fusing them would mean the kernel does: 1. Read (B, L, C), compute mean/var over C, normalize -> this is for norm1 2. Reshape to (B, H, W, C) 3. Pad, window, spatial_mlp, reverse, crop 4. Reshape to (B, L, C) 5. Residual add This is a big kernel but manageable. Actually, LayerNorm needs the weight and bias parameters. Since `self.norm1` is a nn.LayerNorm, we can access its weight and bias. Hmm, but implementing LayerNorm inside a custom kernel is fairly involved because we need to load an entire row (all C channels), compute mean and variance, then normalize. For C up to 768 and B*L up to 31360, this requires careful memory access. For the first stage: B*L = 31360, C = 96. Each row is 96 floats = 384 bytes. A warp (32 threads) can process a row with 3 threads each handling 32 channels. Or each warp can process multiple rows. For stage 4: B*L = 490, C = 768. Each row is 768 floats = 3KB. A warp of 32 threads could process a row with each thread handling 24 channels. Let me think about whether fusing LayerNorm with the spatial_mlp is worth it. Actually, LayerNorm on 31360 rows of 96 floats: - Memory read: 31360 * 96 * 4 Ōēł 12 MB - Memory write: same - Compute: minimal (some adds and multiplies) - Total time at 3.35 TB/s: ~7 microseconds The spatial_mlp for stage 1 we estimated at ~33 microseconds with a custom kernel. So the custom kernel should take (B, H, W, C) and output (B, H, W, C). Let me think about an even simpler approach. What if I just eliminate the `.contiguous()` calls and use `torch.nn.functional.conv1d` more efficiently? In PyTorch, if I can keep the tensor in a layout that's compatible with Conv1d, I can avoid the contiguous() copies. But Conv1d requires the input to be contiguous in (batch, channels, length) layout. And the channels must be the inner dimension before batch in the original (B, H, W, C) tensor... this gets complicated. I think the cleanest high-impact optimization is: 1. Replace spatial_mlp (grouped Conv1d) with a batched matrix multiply 2. Avoid intermediate contiguous() calls by carefully managing tensor layouts For the batched matmul approach: ```python # After window_partition: (nW*B, ws, ws, C) # Instead of .view(-1, ws*ws, C).contiguous() x_reshaped = x_windows.reshape(-1, ws*ws, C) # This might not be contiguous # Then x_windows_heads = x_reshaped.view(-1, ws*ws, num_heads, C//num_heads) # .transpose(1, 2) # (nW*B, num_heads, ws*ws, C//num_heads) # Actually, after window_partition, the tensor IS contiguous with layout: # [nW*B, ws, ws, C] # So x_windows.view(-1, ws*ws, C) IS contiguous # x_windows.view(-1, ws*ws, num_heads, C//num_heads) is also contiguous # .transpose(1, 2) makes it NON-contiguous # Instead of transpose + reshape + conv1d, let's use a different layout ``` I think writing a custom kernel is actually the right approach here. Let me design it properly. Let me write a CUDA kernel that does: 1. Input: (B, H, W, C) tensor 2. For shifted case: conceptually pad, but handle it with boundary checks instead of actual padding 3. Partition into windows 4. For each window: load input, apply spatial_mlp weight, store output 5. For shifted case: unshift the output Wait, I just realized that the pad operation in the reference adds actual zeros. And the spatial_mlp is applied to the padded tensor. If we handle boundaries without explicit padding, we'd need to handle the case where some spatial positions in a window are outside the original image and should be treated as zeros. For the shifted case with cyclic shift or zero padding: - Shifted_x[:, P_t:-P_b, P_l:-P_r, :] = original x - Padded regions are zeros - Spatial_mlp is applied to all windows, including those with pad regions - Then we crop back So in a custom kernel, we can either: 1. Actually pad the tensor (memory copy) 2. Or handle boundary checks when reading the original tensor If we handle boundary checks, we can avoid the pad memory copy. When a window extends beyond the image bounds, those positions read as 0. And for the output, the spatial_mlp result for padded positions is discarded, so we only need to write back to valid positions. This avoids both the pad and the crop operations! So for each window at position (wy, wx) in the padded grid: - The window covers y in [wy*ws, (wy+1)*ws) and x in [wx*ws, (wx+1)*ws) in shifted coordinates - In original coordinates: y in [wy*ws - P_t, (wy+1)*ws - P_t) and x in [wx*ws - P_l, (wx+1)*ws - P_l) - For any position outside [0, H) or [0, W), the input is 0 - After computing the spatial_mlp, we only write back positions where y in [0, H) and x in [0, W) For the non-shifted case, P_t = P_b = P_l = P_r = 0, so no boundary issues. This is actually quite elegant! The custom kernel can handle both shifted and non-shifted cases with the same code, just different boundary parameters. Actually, as I reasoned earlier, we don't need to physically pad or crop. We can just use boundary checks. In the shifted case: - Conceptually, the padded image ranges from (-P_t, -P_l) to (H+P_b, W+P_r) - Each window at grid position (wy, wx) covers: y_start = wy*ws - P_t, y_end = (wy+1)*ws - P_t x_start = wx*ws - P_l, x_end = (wx+1)*ws - P_l But wait, when we partition the padded image, the windows at grid position (wy, wx) in the padded tensor cover: y in [wy*ws, (wy+1)*ws), x in [wx*ws, (wx+1)*ws) Inside the kernel, for a window at grid position (wy, wx): The reading position in the original image is: y_orig = wy*ws - P_t + jy x_orig = wx*ws - P_l + jx So: y_padded = wy*ws + jy x_padded = wx*ws + jx y_orig = y_padded - P_t = wy*ws + jy - P_t x_orig = x_padded - P_l = wx*ws + jx - P_l If y_orig < 0 or y_orig >= H, the input is 0. The zeros at the boundary propagate through the spatial_mlp (since it's a linear transformation of the flattened spatial positions within each head). After window_reverse, we get a tensor of the same size as shifted_x (B, H+ws, W+ws, C). Then we crop out the original region (B, H, W, C). In the custom kernel: 1. Write result to the appropriate position in the output tensor... but wait, the output tensor after spatial_mlp and window reverse has the padded layout. Then we need to crop. Actually, the most natural thing is to write the output of the spatial_mlp directly to the cropped region. Since the padded regions are cropped away, we only need to write windows that overlap the original image. For a window at (wy, wx) in the padded grid: - The window covers y in [wy*ws, (wy+1)*ws), x in [wx*ws, (wx+1)*ws) in padded coordinates - The original image is at y in [P_t, P_t+H), x in [P_l, P_l+W) in padded coordinates - The overlap in padded coordinates is: y_overlap_start = max(wy*ws, P_t) y_overlap_end = min((wy+1)*ws, P_t+H) Similarly for x For positions in the window that overlap with the original image, we write the spatial_mlp output to: y_out = y_padded - P_t = wy*ws + jy - P_t x_out = x_padded - P_l = wx*ws + jx - P_l For positions that don't overlap, we don't need to write anything (they're cropped away). But here's the thing: spatial_mlp is applied to the entire window, including padded positions, because each head transforms all ws*ws spatial positions together. The padded positions (zeros) affect the output because of the matrix multiplication. So for each window, even if only some positions overlap with the original image, we still need to compute the spatial_mlp for all ws*ws positions. However, we only need to store the outputs for positions that fall within the original image bounds. Actually, wait. For a window that's entirely in the padded region (no overlap with original image), all input is 0, so output is also 0. Since this is cropped away, we can skip these windows entirely! For a window that partially overlaps, we need to compute the full spatial_mlp because each output element depends on all input elements in the window. So the optimization is: 1. Only process windows that have at least some overlap with the original image 2. For each such window, compute the spatial_mlp 3. Only write back the output elements that fall within the original image bounds Since the padding is relatively thin (ws = 7), and the typical image sizes are 56x56, 28x28, etc., the number of windows entirely in the padded region is small. For a window grid of (H//ws + 1) x (W//ws + 1) where H=W=56, ws=7: - Grid is 9 x 9 = 81 windows - Original image covers windows that overlap [ws//2, H+ws//2) = [3.5, 59.5)... wait, P_t = ws//2 = 3.5? H+ws = 56+7=63, and P_t+P_b = 4+3=7. So the image is shifted by 3 or 4? Let me look at standard Swin Transformer. shift_size = window_size // 2 = 3 (for window_size=7). Then the image is shifted by 3 positions. But in this code, they use pad instead of roll. P_l = 7 - 3 = 4 P_r = 3 P_t = 4 P_b = 3 This means the original image starts at row 4 and column 4 in the padded tensor. The padded tensor is 63x63. After window partition (window size 7), there are 9x9 windows. - Windows with wy=0, wx=0 cover rows [0,7), cols [0,7). The original image starts at [4,7) in rows and [4,7) in cols. So this window partially overlaps. - Windows with wy=8, wx=8 cover rows [56,63), cols [56,63). The original image ends at row 60 and col 60 (since 56+4=60). So rows [56,60) overlap, cols [56,60) overlap. The windows with wy=0, wx=0 and wy=8, wx=8 are corner cases. Most windows fully or partially overlap. How many windows can we skip? A window at (wy, wx) has NO overlap if: wy*ws >= P_t+H = 4+56 = 60 OR wx*ws >= P_l+W = 60 OR (wy+1)*ws <= P_t = 4 OR (wx+1)*ws <= P_l = 4 For ws=7: - wy*ws >= 60 means wy >= 60/7 Ōēł 8.57, so wy >= 9. So in this case, ALL windows overlap with the original image! The windows at the corners just partially overlap, but they still overlap. For H=56, the window grid spans 0..62 (rows) in the padded tensor. Original image rows are 4..59. Any window row [wy*7, (wy+1)*7) overlaps [4,59) as long as wy*7 < 59 and (wy+1)*7 > 4. For wy=0: [0,7) overlaps [4,59)? Yes, [4,7). For wy=8: [56,63) overlaps [4,59)? Yes, [56,59). So all 9 rows overlap. Similarly for columns. The only case where a window is completely outside is when H < ws or W < ws, but in this model, min(H, W) = 7 = ws in stage 4. For stage 4: H=7, W=7, ws=7. Padded: H+ws=14, W+ws=14, grid is 2x2. Original image at [4, 11) in both dimensions. Window (0,0): [0,7) overlaps [4,11)? Yes. Window (0,1): [0,7) rows, [7,14) cols. Overlaps [4,11)x[7,11)? Yes. Window (1,0): [7,14) rows, [0,7) cols. Overlaps [7,11)x[4,11)? Yes. Window (1,1): [7,14)x[7,14). Overlaps [7,11)x[7,11)? Yes. So again, all windows overlap! This means for shifted cases, we process ALL windows. The only difference is that some input positions are zeros (outside the original image). This makes the kernel logic simpler: we don't need to skip any windows. We just use boundary checks when reading input and when writing output. Actually, for writing output, we only need to write positions that fall within [0, H) x [0, W) in the original image. Let me now think about the kernel design again. I'll write a CUDA kernel for the spatial_mlp path. Let me call it `swin_spatial_mlp_forward`. Kernel parameters: - B, H, W, C - num_heads, ws - shift_size (0 or ws//2) - P_l, P_r, P_t, P_b (derived from shift_size) Grid: each block handles one (b, wy, wx, h) where: - b in [0, B) - wy in [0, _H//ws) - wx in [0, _W//ws) - h in [0, num_heads) Actually, we can batch multiple heads or windows together per block. Let me try a different parallelization. Each block handles one window (b, wy, wx). Inside the block, threads handle all num_heads * ws*ws * C//num_heads output elements. Wait, for a window, we have num_heads independent transformations, each producing ws*ws * C//num_heads output values. For B=10, _H//ws=9, _W//ws=9: total windows = 810. For each window, we need to do num_heads * (ws*ws, ws*ws) x (ws*ws, C//num_heads) = num_heads * (49, 49) x (49, 32) matmuls. With 810 windows and 3..24 heads, total window-head pairs range from 2430 to 19440. If each block handles one window-head pair: - Minimum blocks: 2430 (for stage 1 shifted) - Maximum blocks: 19440 (for stage 4 shifted with 24 heads... wait, stage 4 has H=W=7, ws=7, so grid is 2x2, B=10, num_heads=24. Total windows=40, window-head pairs=960) So the number of blocks ranges from about 1000 to 20000. That's good parallelism. If each block has 32 threads (matching C//num_heads=32), then each thread handles ws*ws=49 output elements for one channel. That's 49 multiply-adds per element per thread * 49 elements = 2401 operations. With 32 threads, total block operations = 76832 FLOPs (multiply-adds). At 67 TFLOPS, this is ~1.1 microseconds per block. For 20000 blocks with 132 SMs: 20000/132 * 1.1 Ōēł 167 microseconds. That's the theoretical minimum for pure compute, but realistically with memory loads it would be more. However, 32 threads per block means only 1 warp. This limits occupancy. On H100, with up to 32 blocks per SM and maybe up to 64 warps per SM, having 1 warp per block would mean at most 32 warps per SM if we have 32 blocks. That's half occupancy at best? Actually, let me not overthink this. To improve occupancy, we can process multiple window-head pairs per block or use more threads per block. Option A: Block size = 256 threads, process 8 window-head pairs per block (32 threads per pair). Total blocks = total_pairs / 8. Option B: Block size = 256 threads, each block handles one window, and all heads. With 24 heads and 256 threads, we could assign ~10 threads per head. Then each thread computes 49/10 Ōēł 5 output elements. Option C: Block size = 1024, one block per window (all heads). 1024 / (num_heads * C//num_heads) = 1024 / (24*32) Ōēł 1.3. Not a whole number. Actually, let me try: - Block size = (C//num_heads, ws*ws) = (32, 49) = 1568 threads. That's too many. Or: - Block size = (C//num_heads, 16) = (32, 16) = 512 threads - Grid: (num_windows, num_heads/16 rounded up) - Each block handles one window and up to 16 heads - For 24 heads, we'd have 2 blocks per window (16 heads + 8 heads) For stage 1: 810 windows, 3 heads. Each block handles 1 window and 3 heads. Block size = 32 * 49 = 1568... too big. Actually, since C//num_heads = 32 always, and ws*ws = 49 always, the work per window-head pair is fixed at 1568 output elements. What if block size is 128? Then each block handles 128/32 = 4 "channel columns" across different heads and/or spatial positions. Hmm, let me simplify. I'll use 1D thread blocks of 256 threads. Each thread computes multiple output elements. For a window-head pair with 1568 output elements (49 spatial * 32 channels): - 256 threads compute 1568/256 Ōēł 6.1 elements each. So 7 elements for some threads. - Or 128 threads compute ~12 elements each. - Or 64 threads compute ~24 elements each. With 64 threads (2 warps), each thread computes: - A range of output channels: e.g., thread c handles channels [c_start, c_end) - And a range of spatial positions: [o_start, o_end) A clean assignment: - Thread index = threadIdx.x - Total outputs = 49 * 32 = 1568 - Each thread computes outputs at positions: threadIdx.x, threadIdx.x + 64, threadIdx.x + 128, ... up to 1568 So thread i computes outputs [i, i+64, i+128, i+192, i+256, i+320, i+384, i+448, i+512, i+576, i+640, i+704, i+768, i+832, i+896, i+960, i+1024, i+1088, i+1152, i+1216, i+1280, i+1344, i+1408, i+1472] That's 24 outputs per thread. For each output index idx, we compute: o = idx / 32 (spatial position, 0..48) c = idx % 32 (channel, 0..31) output[o, c] = sum_i W[o, i] * input[i, c] Each thread needs to load: - W_shared: all 49*49 floats (shared by all threads in block) - input_shared: all 49*32 floats (shared by all threads in block) Wait, but input depends on the window's position in the original tensor. Since each block handles one window-head pair, the input for that window-head pair is in the global tensor at specific locations. To load input into shared memory: - We need to read 49 spatial positions, each with 32 channels - Total 1568 floats = 6.2 KB - With 64 threads, each thread loads 1568/64 = 24.5 Ōēł 25 values When loading from global memory, for spatial position i and channel c: y_in = ... (depends on window position and i) x_in = ... if inside original image: val = input[b, y_in, x_in, h*32 + c] else: val = 0 The memory access pattern depends on how we map threadIdx.x to (i, c). If thread i loads a contiguous chunk of the 1568 elements, say elements [start, end), then consecutive threads access consecutive memory locations if the elements are stored consecutively in global memory. In the (B, _H, _W, num_heads*32) layout, the elements for window (wy, wx) and head h are at: - y_padded = wy*ws + jy, x_padded = wx*ws + jx - j = jy*ws + jx is the spatial position - Channel c in [0, 32) - Global index: b*_H*_W*C + (wy*ws + jy)*_W*C + (wx*ws + jx)*C + h*32 + c For fixed (b, wy, wx, h) and varying (jy, jx, c), the memory layout is not contiguous for varying jy because of the _W*C stride. For varying jx with fixed jy and c, the stride is C. For varying c with fixed jy and jx, the stride is 1. So if a thread reads a contiguous range of output indices and maps them to (j, c), the memory access is strided by C for varying jx, and strided by _W*C for varying jy. This is not very coalesced. To get coalesced access, we should have threads in a warp read consecutive c values for the same (jy, jx). Since C=32, a warp of 32 threads can read all 32 channels for one spatial position j. So cooperative loading: - Thread i in [0, 32) loads channel i for spatial position j = block_group - We can divide the 49 spatial positions into groups and load them cooperatively But 49 is not a multiple of warp size. Hmm. Alternative: use 32 threads where thread c loads all 49 spatial positions for channel c, one at a time. This is sequential but coalesced within each spatial position (since 32 threads read 32 consecutive channels). Actually, let's do this: - Block has 32 threads (one per channel) - Thread c reads input[spatial_pos, c] for spatial_pos = 0..48 - For a given spatial_pos, threads 0..31 read channels 0..31, which are consecutive in memory (since they're at ... + h*32 + c where c varies). - This is perfectly coalesced! - Total loads: 49 iterations, each with 32 coalesced loads. Similarly for writing output: - Thread c writes output[spatial_pos, c] for spatial_pos = 0..48 - 49 iterations, each with 32 coalesced stores. For the computation: - Each thread c computes all 49 output values for its channel: output[o, c] = sum_i W[o, i] * input[i, c] - To do this, W[o, i] for all o, i is needed by all threads in the block. - We can load W into shared memory cooperatively: 49*49 = 2401 floats = ~9.6KB - Or keep W in constant memory / registers Wait, but W is different for each head. Since each block handles one window-head pair, W for that head can be loaded into shared memory. Loading W into shared memory: - 2401 floats, with 32 threads: each thread loads 2401/32 Ōēł 75 floats - Access pattern: W[h, o, i] stored as (num_heads, 49, 49) - For head h, W[h, :, :] is a contiguous 2401-float chunk (assuming row-major for the last two dims) - So loading is coalesced if threads read consecutive elements. Then the computation: - Thread c loops over o = 0..48: sum = 0 for i = 0..48: sum += W_shared[o, i] * input_shared[i, c] output_shared[o, c] = sum - Then write output to global memory. Each thread does 49 * 49 = 2401 multiply-adds. At ~4 FLOPs/cycle per CUDA core (rough estimate), and H100 having ~1400 MHz clock with many cores... actually, H100 SMs have high throughput for FP32 FMA. For this tiny matmul, performance is probably limited by memory bandwidth for loading input and W, and by instruction throughput for the FMAs. Let's compute memory traffic per block: - Load input: 49*32*4 bytes = 6.2 KB - Load W: 49*49*4 bytes = 9.6 KB - Store output: 49*32*4 bytes = 6.2 KB - Total R+W: ~22 KB At 3.35 TB/s, 22 KB takes ~6.6 nanoseconds. But with cache effects, the W matrix might stay in L1 cache if reused. And since we're not streaming through a large dataset, this should be mostly L1/L2 bandwidth. Actually for 20000 blocks, the total memory traffic for input+output is 20000 * 12.4KB Ōēł 248MB. The weights for all heads are small (24 heads * 9.6KB Ōēł 230KB). So total memory is well under a few hundred MB, which is small. The main limitation is likely instruction throughput and kernel launch overhead. With 32 threads per block, we launch many blocks with few threads. Let's see occupancy limits for H100: - Max 32 blocks per SM - Max 64 warps per SM (for A100; H100 might be similar or higher) - With 32 threads = 1 warp per block, 32 blocks per SM = 32 warps per SM - This might be below the max warps per SM, so occupancy is limited. To get more warps per SM and better utilization, we should increase threads per block. What about 128 threads (4 warps) per block, processing 4 window-head pairs? Each window-head pair still has 1568 outputs, and we assign 32 threads per pair. Total blocks = total_pairs / 4. For stage 1 shifted: 810 windows * 3 heads = 2430 pairs. 2430/4 Ōēł 608 blocks. For stage 4 shifted: 40 windows * 24 heads = 960 pairs. 960/4 = 240 blocks. 608 blocks across 132 SMs gives ~4.6 blocks per SM. With 4 warps each, that's ~18 warps per SM. What if I just restructure the PyTorch operations to be more efficient, and only write custom kernels for the highest-impact fusions? And what if I avoid the `contiguous()` calls by carefully using `reshape` instead of `view` where needed, and by reordering operations? In PyTorch, `view` only works when the tensor is contiguous in the required layout. `reshape` can return a view or a copy, whichever is needed. But `permute` always makes the tensor non-contiguous. For window_partition: ```python x = x.view(B, H // window_size, window_size, W // window_size, window_size, C) windows = x.permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5).contiguous().view(-1, window_size, window_size, C) ``` The permute changes the memory layout, so contiguous() copies. But maybe we can avoid the contiguous() if the next operation doesn't require it? Looking at the next step: `x_windows.view(-1, self.window_size * self.window_size, C)`. A view requires contiguous memory, but after permute, the tensor is NOT contiguous. So we need contiguous(). Unless... we change the layout. If we keep the tensor in (B, nH, nW, ws, ws, C) layout, then: - x_windows.view(-1, ws*ws, C) requires the (ws, ws, C) part to be contiguous. Since C varies fastest and ws*ws*C is contiguous in this layout, it should work! Wait, let me check. After `windows = x.permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5)`: The new shape is (B, nH, nW, ws, ws, C). The stride is: (H*W*C, W*ws*C, ws*C, C, W*C, 1)... no wait. Original stride after `x.view(B, nH, ws, nW, ws, C)`: If x was (B, H, W, C) contiguous with stride (H*W*C, W*C, C, 1): After view(B, nH, ws, nW, ws, C), stride is (nH*ws*nW*ws*C, ws*nW*ws*C, nW*ws*C, ws*C, C, 1). After permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5), the new dimensions are: 0: B, stride = nH*ws*nW*ws*C 1: nH, stride = ws*nW*ws*C 2: nW, stride = ws*C 3: ws, stride = nW*ws*C 4: ws, stride = C 5: C, stride = 1 So for a fixed (b, nH, nW), the slice (ws, ws, C) has strides (nW*ws*C, C, 1). This means varying the last ws (dim 4) has stride C, varying C has stride 1. For `view(-1, ws, ws, C)` on this permuted tensor: the last 3 dimensions have strides (nW*ws*C, C, 1) and sizes (ws, ws, C). But ws*ws*C elements are not contiguous because the stride from the first ws to the second ws is nW*ws*C, not ws*C. Wait, dim 3 has size ws and stride nW*ws*C. Dim 4 has size ws and stride C. So moving from element (i, j, k) within the (ws, ws, C) slice to (i+1, j, k) jumps by nW*ws*C, not by ws*C. This means we can't view the last 3 dims as a single dimension without a copy. So contiguous() IS necessary. But what if we rewrite window_partition differently? If we reshape the original tensor from (B, H, W, C) to (B, nH, ws, nW, ws, C), the stride is (H*W*C, W*ws*C, W*C, ws*C, C, 1). Wait no. Original contiguous (B, H, W, C): stride = (H*W*C, W*C, C, 1) After view(B, nH, ws, nW, ws, C): stride = (H*W*C, W*C*ws? No. `view` preserves the total number of elements and the contiguous order. So: - B dimension: stride H*W*C - nH dimension: stride = ws*nW*ws*C = H*W*C / nH = W*ws*C... wait. - B stride: H*W*C - nH stride: ws * nW * ws * C = ws * (W/ws) * ws * C = W * ws * C - ws stride: nW * ws * C = (W/ws) * ws * C = W * C - nW stride: ws * C - ws stride: C - C stride: 1 So after view(B, nH, ws, nW, ws, C), the strides are: (H*W*C, W*ws*C, W*C, ws*C, C, 1) Then permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5) gives: - B: stride H*W*C - nH: stride W*ws*C - nW: stride ws*C - ws: stride W*C - ws: stride C - C: stride 1 The last 3 dims (ws, ws, C) have strides (W*C, C, 1). For these to be viewable as a single dimension, we need the first ws * second stride = W*C and the last two strides to satisfy ws*C = total_size of last two dims * their stride. Well, W*C = ws * (W/ws) * C = ws * nW * C. But we want the first ws dimension to have stride equal to the total size of the remaining dims (ws*C) for them to be contiguous. Since stride of first ws is W*C and we want it to be ws*C, we need W = ws, which is not true in general. So indeed, we need a copy to get contiguous windows. The fundamental issue is that the window partition inherently requires reordering memory. A custom kernel can operate on the non-contiguous layout directly. OK, I think writing the custom kernel for the spatial_mlp path is the right approach. Let me design it. Given the complexity, let me write a relatively simple kernel that handles the core operation without extreme optimization. Since I have 8 turns, I need to balance complexity with correctness. Let me also think about whether I can make the overall model simpler. What if I trace through the model and identify operations that can be batched or merged? Actually, one more insight: the spatial_mlp weight matrix is always (num_heads, 49, 49). And the operation is a matrix multiplication for each window and head. On H100, I can use tensor cores! But the matrices are too small (49x49) to get good tensor core utilization. What if I batch across windows and channels? For stage 1 non-shifted: - Input per head: (640, 49, 32) - We want: (640, 49, 32) = W[head] @ (640, 49, 32) but along the right dimension. Actually, if we view input as (640*32, 49) and want output as (640*32, 49) with output[i] = input[i] @ W[head].T: For fixed head h: - input_h: (640, 49, 32) - Reshape to (640*32, 49) = (20480, 49) - W_h.T: (49, 49) - Output: (20480, 49) @ (49, 49) = (20480, 49) - Reshape to (640, 49, 32) This is just one matrix multiplication per head! And (20480, 49) @ (49, 49) is a decent-sized matmul that should use cuBLAS efficiently. For stage 1 shifted: B*nWH*nWW = 10*9*9 = 810. Per head: (810, 49, 32) -> (810*32, 49) = (25920, 49). @ (49, 49) -> (25920, 49). This is even larger! cuBLAS should handle this well. For stage 4: 40 windows per head. (40*32, 49) = (1280, 49). @ (49, 49). This is smaller but still a single matmul call. So the approach is: 1. For each head, extract the input for all windows 2. Reshape to (num_windows * C//num_heads, ws*ws) 3. Bmm with W[head] transposed 4. Reshape back And I can do this without the grouped Conv1d, which might be slower due to group overheads. But still, the window partition and reverse require copies. Unless my custom kernel handles them directly. Actually, for this batched matmul approach, I can minimize intermediate copies by: 1. Not doing explicit window_partition with copy 2. Instead, create a strided tensor that represents the windows In PyTorch, `as_strided` can create a view without copy! Let me think about this. Input x is (B, H, W, C) contiguous with stride (H*W*C, W*C, C, 1). I want windows of size (ws, ws) at positions (b, wy, wx). Using `as_strided`: ```python # x shape: (B, H, W, C) # We want windows as (B, nH, nW, ws, ws, C) x_windows = x.as_strided( size=(B, nH, nW, ws, ws, C), stride=(H*W*C, W*ws*C, ws*C, W*C, C, 1) ) ``` Wait, let's verify the stride. For a window at (b, wy, wx), position (jy, jx, c): offset = b*H*W*C + wy*W*ws*C + jy*W*C + wx*ws*C + jx*C + c In the 6D tensor (B, nH, nW, ws, ws, C): - b stride: H*W*C - wy stride: W*ws*C (moving to next row of windows) - wx stride: ws*C (moving to next column of windows) - jy stride: W*C (moving down one row within window) - jx stride: C (moving right one col within window) - c stride: 1 This gives the correct offsets! And `as_strided` returns a view without copy. Then we can reshape this to separate heads and channels: ```python x_windows = x_windows.reshape(B, nH, nW, ws, ws, num_heads, C//num_heads) # transpose to move heads before window dims x_windows = x_windows.permute(0, 5, 1, 2, 3, 4, 6) # shape: (B, num_heads, nH, nW, ws, ws, C//num_heads) ``` This permute makes the tensor non-contiguous in a way that might not be ideal. Alternatively: ```python x_windows = x.as_strided( size=(B, nH, nW, ws, ws, num_heads, C//num_heads), stride=(H*W*C, W*ws*C, ws*C, W*C, C, C//num_heads, 1) ) # This gives a 7D view! # Then we can reshape to what we need: x_windows = x_windows.permute(0, 5, 1, 2, 3, 4, 6) # shape: (B, num_heads, nH, nW, ws, ws, C//num_heads) ``` Now for the matmul, we want per-head: input shape: (B*nH*nW, ws*ws, C//num_heads) So we reshape: ```python x_for_mm = x_windows.reshape(B, num_heads, -1, ws*ws, C//num_heads) # Then for each head: # x_head = x_for_mm[:, h, :, :, :] # (B, nW_total, ws*ws, C//num_heads) # reshape to (B*nW_total*C//num_heads, ws*ws) ``` Wait, the actual bmm needs input as (B*nW_total * C//num_heads, ws*ws) @ (ws*ws, ws*ws). Let me think about this more carefully. For head h: x_head = x_windows[:, h, :, :, :, :] # (B, nH, nW, ws, ws, C//num_heads) Reshape to (B*nH*nW, ws*ws, C//num_heads) Then transpose to (B*nH*nW, C//num_heads, ws*ws) Reshape to (B*nH*nW*C//num_heads, ws*ws) Matmul with (ws*ws, ws*ws): result = (B*nH*nW*C//num_heads, ws*ws) @ (ws*ws, ws*ws) = (B*nH*nW*C//num_heads, ws*ws) Reshape to (B*nH*nW, C//num_heads, ws*ws) Transpose to (B*nH*nW, ws*ws, C//num_heads) Reshape to (B, nH, nW, ws, ws, C//num_heads) Then we need to combine across heads: result = result.reshape(B, nH, nW, ws, ws, num_heads, C//num_heads) # permute to match original layout and write back But writing back is tricky because we're handling shifted and non-shifted cases. For the non-shifted case, output positions are: y = x.as_strided(size=(B, nH, nW, ws, ws, C), stride=...) Then we write to the same positions. For the shifted case, the output needs to be written to the original positions after the shift. This is more complex because each window in the padded tensor corresponds to a specific region in the original tensor. Actually, I think custom CUDA is still the cleanest solution for the spatial_mlp path. Let me now design a custom CUDA kernel for the spatial_mlp + window reverse. I'll create a kernel that: 1. Takes input tensor (B, _H, _W, C) where _H and _W are the padded dimensions if shifted 2. But wait, if we want to avoid padding, we should pass the original (B, H, W, C) and handle padding implicitly. Kernel signature: ```cpp void swin_spatial_mlp_forward( const float* input, // (B, H, W, C) - original image float* output, // (B, H, W, C) - output after spatial_mlp and unshift const float* weight, // (num_heads, ws*ws, ws*ws) const float* bias, // (num_heads, ws*ws) or nullptr int B, int H, int W, int C, int num_heads, int ws, int shift_size ); ``` In the kernel, each block handles one window position in the padded grid and one head. For window (b, wy, wx) in padded grid, head h: 1. For each spatial position j in [0, ws*ws): Compute jy = j / ws, jx = j % ws y_padded = wy * ws + jy x_padded = wx * ws + jx y_orig = y_padded - P_t (where P_t = ws - shift_size if shift_size > 0 else 0) x_orig = x_padded - P_l For each channel c in [0, C//num_heads): if 0 <= y_orig < H and 0 <= x_orig < W: in_val = input[b, y_orig, x_orig, h*C//num_heads + c] else: in_val = 0.0f Store in_val in shared memory 2. Synchronize 3. Load W for this head into shared memory 4. For each output spatial position o and channel c: out_val = 0 for j in [0, ws*ws): out_val += W[h, o, j] * input_shared[j, c] if bias: out_val += bias[h, o] 5. Write output to appropriate positions in output tensor: y_padded = wy * ws + o / ws x_padded = wy * ws + o % ws y_orig = y_padded - P_t x_orig = x_padded - P_l if 0 <= y_orig < H and 0 <= x_orig < W: output[b, y_orig, x_orig, h*C//num_heads + c] = out_val else: discard (this is the padded region that's cropped) Wait, but step 1 has a loop over j and c, which is 49*32 = 1568 values per window-head pair. And step 4 and 5 also iterate over outputs. Each block handling one window-head pair: - 32 threads, each handling one channel - Thread c loads input[j, c] for j=0..48 (coalesced: threads 0..31 read channels 0..31 for fixed j) - Thread c computes output[o, c] for o=0..48 - Thread c writes output[o, c] for o=0..48 For step 1 (loading input): Loop j=0..48: jy = j / 7, jx = j % 7 y_padded = wy*7 + jy x_padded = wx*7 + jx y_orig = y_padded - P_t x_orig = x_padded - P_l offset = b*H*W*C + y_orig*W*C + x_orig*C + h*32 + threadIdx.x if (valid) smem[j*32 + threadIdx.x] = input[offset] else smem[j*32 + threadIdx.x] = 0 This is coalesced because all threads in the warp read consecutive memory (h*32 + threadIdx.x varies by 1 per thread). Wait, but x_orig varies with jx, so for different j, the base offset changes by W*C or C. But within each j, threads read consecutive memory. So across the loop iterations j=0..48, each iteration has coalesced access. Similarly for writing output: Loop o=0..48: if valid output position: offset = b*H*W*C + y_out*W*C + x_out*C + h*32 + threadIdx.x output[offset] = out_val Coalesced! This is actually very good. For W loading: W is at weight + h*49*49. We need to load 49*49 = 2401 floats into shared memory. With 32 threads: each loads 2401/32 Ōēł 75 floats. Coalesced since W for each head is contiguous. And for computation: Thread c computes out[o, c] = sum_j W[o, j] * in[j, c] We can load W into shared memory as W_smem[49][49]. And input into shared memory as in_smem[49][32]. Then: for o = 0..48: sum = 0 for j = 0..48: sum += W_smem[o][j] * in_smem[j][threadIdx.x] out_smem[o][threadIdx.x] = sum + bias_smem[o] Each thread does 49*49 = 2401 FMAs. This is enough to amortize the shared memory latency if we unroll the loop or if the compiler does it. But wait, there's a memory dependency: W_smem[o][j] is accessed sequentially. If W_smem is in shared memory, this is fast. And in_smem[j][c] for fixed c varies with j, which might have bank conflicts if not careful. Shared memory banks: H100 has 32 banks. in_smem[j][c] where c = threadIdx.x. For a fixed c, consecutive j values map to the same bank? Actually, shared memory is banked by address, not by the 2D indexing. If we store in_smem as float in_smem[49][32], then in_smem[j][c] is at address (j*32 + c) * sizeof(float). Since 32 floats = 128 bytes, and each bank is 4 bytes, consecutive j values with the same c map to the same bank (j*32*4 mod 128 = 0). Oh no! This means all threads with the same c but different j hit the same bank when c is the thread index. Wait, thread c accesses in_smem[j][c]. Thread c has fixed c across the j loop. For different j: address = base + j*32*4 + c*4 = base + j*128 + c*4 bank = (address / 4) % 32 = (j*32 + c) % 32 = c % 32 So all accesses for a fixed c go to bank c! But since each thread has a DIFFERENT c, and threads in a warp have c = 0..31, they all access DIFFERENT banks. This is actually PERFECT! No bank conflicts at all. Similarly for W_smem[o][j]: all threads in the warp read the same W_smem[o][j] at the same time. This is a broadcast, which is also efficient in shared memory. So the memory access pattern is excellent! OK, I think this kernel design is solid. Let me also think about: 1. Do I need to handle fp16? The problem says fp32 precision. 2. What about bias? The Conv1d might have bias. Looking at the reference: `self.spatial_mlp = nn.Conv1d(...)` with no explicit bias parameter, so bias defaults to True in PyTorch Conv1d. Actually, I'll make it dynamic: ```cpp int channels_per_head = C / num_heads; int threads = min(channels_per_head, 128); // or some heuristic ``` And then have each thread handle `channels_per_head / threads` channels. If it's not evenly divisible, the last thread handles the remainder. But for this specific problem, channels_per_head is always 32, so I'll just use 32 threads per block for simplicity, and document that it works for multiples of 32. Hmm, but I want the kernel to be correct for any valid parameters. Let me use a grid-stride loop for generality. Actually, let me look at the kernel size: in the reference model, window_size is fixed at 7. But the constructor takes it as an argument. If someone passes window_size=8, ws*ws=64 and the shared memory for W would be 64*64*4=16KB. Still fine. So let me make ws a parameter too. Let me start drafting the kernel. I'll use a 1D block where each thread handles a subset of channels for one (window, head) pair. CUDA kernel design: ```cuda __global__ void swin_spatial_mlp_kernel( const float* input, float* output, const float* weight, const float* bias, int B, int H, int W, int C, int num_heads, int ws, int shift_size ) { // Grid: (num_windows_x, num_windows_y, B) for spatial, blocks for heads // Actually, better: each block handles one (b, wy, wx, h) int total_windows_h = (H + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0) + ws - 1) / ws; // No, padded dimensions // Actually _H = H + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0) in our implicit padding int _H = H + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0); int _W = W + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0); int nWH = _H / ws; int nWW = _W / ws; int pair_idx = blockIdx.x; // Each block is one (b, wy, wx, h) pair // Or we can use 3D grid: (nWW, nWH, B) and process all heads in the block // Let me use: blockIdx.x for window index, blockIdx.y for head index int window_idx = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int b = window_idx / (nWH * nWW); int rem = window_idx % (nWH * nWW); int wy = rem / nWW; int wx = rem % nWW; if (b >= B || h >= num_heads) return; int P_t = (shift_size > 0) ? (ws - shift_size) : 0; int P_l = (shift_size > 0) ? (ws - shift_size) : 0; int channels_per_head = C / num_heads; int ws_sq = ws * ws; // Shared memory: W (ws_sq * ws_sq floats), input (ws_sq * channels_per_head floats) // We allocate dynamically based on channels_per_head extern __shared__ float shared_mem[]; float* W_shared = shared_mem; float* in_shared = shared_mem + ws_sq * ws_sq; float* out_shared = in_shared + ws_sq * channels_per_head; // optional, could write directly // Load W for this head const float* W_ptr = weight + h * ws_sq * ws_sq; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < ws_sq * ws_sq; i += blockDim.x) { W_shared[i] = W_ptr[i]; } // Load input for (int j = 0; j < ws_sq; j++) { int jy = j / ws; int jx = j % ws; int y_padded = wy * ws + jy; int x_padded = wx * ws + jx; int y_orig = y_padded - P_t; int x_orig = x_padded - P_l; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < channels_per_head; c += blockDim.x) { float val = 0.0f; if (y_orig >= 0 && y_orig < H && x_orig >= 0 && x_orig < W) { int idx = b * H * W * C + y_orig * W * C + x_orig * C + h * channels_per_head + c; val = input[idx]; } in_shared[j * channels_per_head + c] = val; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute output for (int o = 0; o < ws_sq; o++) { for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < channels_per_head; c += blockDim.x) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < ws_sq; j++) { sum += W_shared[o * ws_sq + j] * in_shared[j * channels_per_head + c]; } if (bias != nullptr) { sum += bias[h * ws_sq + o]; } // Write to output if inside original image int oy = o / ws; int ox = o % ws; int y_padded = wy * ws + oy; int x_padded = wx * ws + ox; int y_orig = y_padded - P_t; int x_orig = x_padded - P_l; if (y_orig >= 0 && y_orig < H && x_orig >= 0 && x_orig < W) { int idx = b * H * W * C + y_orig * W * C + x_orig * C + h * channels_per_head + c; output[idx] = sum; } } } } ``` Wait, this has some issues: 1. The loops over `j` and `o` have `__syncthreads()` implicitly not needed, but all threads in the block need to finish loading before computing. The `__syncthreads()` is there. 2. The output writes: different `o` values might write to the same memory location? No, each `o` is a different spatial position. 3. For a given (b, wy, wx, h), we process all ws*ws spatial positions. Multiple windows might write to the same output pixel? No, window partition covers each padded pixel exactly once. 4. Wait, in the shifted case, the padded grid has size H+ws by W+ws. But the original image is only H by W. For output, we only write pixels that map to the original image. Each original pixel is covered by exactly one window in the padded grid, so no race conditions. Actually, let me verify. For a pixel at (y, x) in the original image, in the padded tensor it maps to (y+P_t, x+P_l). This pixel belongs to window: wy = (y+P_t) / ws wx = (x+P_l) / ws And within this window, the spatial position is: o_y = (y+P_t) % ws o_x = (x+P_l) % ws o = o_y * ws + o_x So each original pixel is written by exactly one block! No race conditions. For pixels in the padded region (outside original image), we don't write them. This is correct! Now, the block dimensions: For stages where the number of heads is small (3) but windows are many (640 for stage 1): - Total pairs = 640 * 3 = 1920 - If I use 1D grid of 1920 blocks with blockDim.x = channels_per_head = 32 - This gives 1920 blocks of 32 threads each For stage 4 with 24 heads and 40 windows: - Total pairs = 960 - Grid: 960 blocks * 32 threads Grid launch: ```cpp int nWH = _H / ws; int nWW = _W / ws; int num_windows = B * nWH * nWW; dim3 grid(num_windows, num_heads); dim3 block(channels_per_head); ``` Wait, the maximum block size is 1024. channels_per_head=32 is fine. The maximum grid dimensions are huge (2^31-1 for x, 65535 for y, 65535 for z). num_windows can be up to B * nWH * nWW. For B=10, _H=63, ws=7, nWH=9, nWW=9, num_windows=810. For y dimension (num_heads), max is 24. Both well within limits. For stage 1 non-shifted: num_windows=10*8*8=640, num_heads=3. grid=(640, 3). For stage 1 shifted: grid=(810, 3). Shared memory size per block: ws*ws*ws*sq floats for W + ws*sq*channels_per_head floats for input. With ws=7, ws_sq=49, channels_per_head=32: W: 49*49*4 = 9604 bytes Input: 49*32*4 = 6272 bytes Output: We don't need output shared memory, we write directly to global. Total shared: ~16KB. Well under limits. If channels_per_head=64 and ws=14 (hypothetical): W: 14^4 * 4 = 38416 bytes Input: 14*14*64*4 = 50176 bytes Total: ~88KB. Still under 228KB. But the more shared memory per block, the fewer blocks can run concurrently on an SM. At 16KB, we can have many concurrent blocks. Actually, I just realized that in my kernel, I didn't use `out_shared`. I write directly to global memory. That's fine. Let me also check if `bias` is used correctly. Actually, PyTorch's `load_inline` works as follows: if `cuda_sources` is provided, the `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources` are combined and passed to nvcc. The file gets compiled with nvcc because it contains CUDA code. Actually, wait - does PyTorch detect CUDA code in `cpp_sources` and automatically use nvcc? I think yes, if the system has CUDA and PyTorch was compiled with CUDA. But there's a subtlety: if `cpp_sources` are treated as C++ files (.cpp extension), the `<<< >>>` syntax would fail. PyTorch's `load_inline` might use the `.cu` extension or compile with nvcc when CUDA is available. Looking at the implementation of `load_inline`, it typically writes the sources to temp files and uses `load` to compile them. The `load` function checks if CUDA is available and uses nvcc accordingly. For maximum compatibility, I'll put the CUDA wrapper in `cpp_sources` but name the file `.cu` extension... Actually, `load_inline` handles all of this. Let me just follow common patterns. Common pattern from PyTorch tutorials: ```python custom_op = load_inline( name="custom_op", cpp_sources=""" #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor my_op(torch::Tensor x) { return x * 2; } """, functions=["my_op"] ) ``` For CUDA: ```python custom_op = load_inline( name="custom_cuda_op", cpp_sources=""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void kernel(float* x, int n) { int i = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (i < n) x[i] *= 2; } torch::Tensor my_op(torch::Tensor x) { auto n = x.numel(); kernel<<<(n+255)/256, 256>>>(x.data_ptr<float>(), n); return x; } """, functions=["my_op"] ) ``` Wait, does `cpp_sources` get compiled with nvcc in this case? I think yes, because PyTorch detects the CUDA runtime include. But to be absolutely safe, I can use the `cuda_sources` parameter for the CUDA-specific code: ```python ext = load_inline( name="swin_ext", cpp_sources=r''' #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor swin_spatial_mlp_forward(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, int shift_size); ''', cuda_sources=r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void kernel(...) { ... } torch::Tensor swin_spatial_mlp_forward(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, int shift_size) { ... } ''', functions=["swin_spatial_mlp_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"], verbose=False, ) ``` I think this is the correct pattern. `cpp_sources` contains the declaration, `cuda_sources` contains the implementation, and `functions` lists what to bind. Actually, I just realized something. The problem says: "Do not write your own `PYBIND11_MODULE(...)` block in `cpp_sources`. `load_inline` auto-generates one from the `functions=[...]` argument; including your own causes a duplicate-symbol redefinition error at compile time." So `load_inline` generates the pybind11 module. The functions in `functions=[...]` are the names of the C++ functions that will be exposed. My `cpp_sources` should NOT include a PYBIND11_MODULE. Here's my full kernel: ```cuda #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void swin_spatial_mlp_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int H, int W, int C, int num_heads, int ws, int shift_size ) { int _H = H + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0); int _W = W + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0); int nWH = _H / ws; int nWW = _W / ws; int window_idx = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; if (window_idx >= B * nWH * nWW || h >= num_heads) return; int b = window_idx / (nWH * nWW); int rem = window_idx % (nWH * nWW); int wy = rem / nWW; int wx = rem % nWW; int P_t = (shift_size > 0) ? (ws - shift_size) : 0; int P_l = (shift_size > 0) ? (ws - shift_size) : 0; int channels_per_head = C / num_heads; int ws_sq = ws * ws; extern __shared__ float shared_mem[]; float* W_shared = shared_mem; float* in_shared = shared_mem + ws_sq * ws_sq; const float* W_ptr = weight + h * ws_sq * ws_sq; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < ws_sq * ws_sq; i += blockDim.x) { W_shared[i] = W_ptr[i]; } for (int j = 0; j < ws_sq; j++) { int jy = j / ws; int jx = j % ws; int y_padded = wy * ws + jy; int x_padded = wx * ws + jx; int y_orig = y_padded - P_t; int x_orig = x_padded - P_l; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < channels_per_head; c += blockDim.x) { float val = 0.0f; if (y_orig >= 0 && y_orig < H && x_orig >= 0 && x_orig < W) { int idx = ((b * H + y_orig) * W + x_orig) * C + h * channels_per_head + c; val = input[idx]; } in_shared[j * channels_per_head + c] = val; } } __syncthreads(); for (int o = 0; o < ws_sq; o++) { for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < channels_per_head; c += blockDim.x) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < ws_sq; j++) { sum += W_shared[o * ws_sq + j] * in_shared[j * channels_per_head + c]; } if (bias != nullptr) { sum += bias[h * ws_sq + o]; } int oy = o / ws; int ox = o % ws; int y_padded = wy * ws + oy; int x_padded = wx * ws + ox; int y_orig = y_padded - P_t; int x_orig = x_padded - P_l; if (y_orig >= 0 && y_orig < H && x_orig >= 0 && x_orig < W) { int idx = ((b * H + y_orig) * W + x_orig) * C + h * channels_per_head + c; output[idx] = sum; } } } } torch::Tensor swin_spatial_mlp_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, int shift_size ) { auto B = input.size(0); auto H = input.size(1); auto W = input.size(2); auto C = input.size(3); auto num_heads = weight.size(0); auto ws_sq = weight.size(1); auto ws = (int)sqrt((float)ws_sq); int _H = H + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0); int _W = W + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0); int nWH = _H / ws; int nWW = _W / ws; auto output = torch::empty_like(input); int num_windows = B * nWH * nWW; dim3 grid(num_windows, num_heads); int channels_per_head = C / num_heads; int block_size = channels_per_head; if (block_size > 512) block_size = 512; if (block_size < 32) block_size = 32; int shared_mem_size = ws_sq * ws_sq * sizeof(float) + ws_sq * channels_per_head * sizeof(float); swin_spatial_mlp_kernel<<<grid, block_size, shared_mem_size>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), (bias.defined() && bias.numel() > 0) ? bias.data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, B, H, W, C, num_heads, ws, shift_size ); return output; } ``` Wait, there's a bug in my kernel loading logic. `block_size = channels_per_head;` but if `channels_per_head` is not a power of 2 or not aligned with warp size, some threads will be idle.
