In my kernel, W is accessed as `W_shared[o * ws_sq + j]` for varying j. This is a row access, which is fine. And `in_shared[j * channels_per_head + c]` for fixed c and varying j. This accesses elements with stride `channels_per_head`. If `channels_per_head = 32`, then `j * 32 + c` accesses banks `c` mod 32 for varying j, which means consecutive j values for the same thread hit the same bank. But since each thread has a different `c`, threads in the warp access different banks. This is actually ideal for broadcast-like access? Wait, when thread c reads `in_shared[j * 32 + c]` and thread c' reads `in_shared[j * 32 + c']`, the addresses are `j*128 + c*4` and `j*128 + c'*4`. The bank is `(j*32 + c) % 32` and `(j*32 + c') % 32 = c' % 32`. So different threads in the warp access different banks - NO bank conflict! But when all threads read the same j, they access different banked addresses simultaneously. That's perfect! When iterating j, each thread advances by 32 in bank index, which wraps around to the same bank. So for a fixed thread c, consecutive j accesses hit the same bank. Since only one thread accesses that bank, there's no conflict, but it doesn't benefit from bank-level parallelism. Still, a single thread accessing one bank repeatedly is fine. So the memory access pattern is excellent. No bank conflicts for in_shared. For W_shared, all threads read `W_shared[o * ws_sq + j]` for the same j at the same time (or close to it). Since `o` might vary across threads, the access pattern depends on how I parallelize the o loop. In my kernel: ```cuda for (int o = 0; o < ws_sq; o++) { for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < channels_per_head; c += blockDim.x) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < ws_sq; j++) { sum += W_shared[o * ws_sq + j] * in_shared[j * channels_per_head + c]; } ``` Here, for a given `o`, all threads with the same `threadIdx.x` but potentially different `blockIdx` (not in the same block) are irrelevant. Within a block, all threads iterate over `o` in lockstep (since there's no thread divergence in the j loop). And for a given `o` and `j`, all threads read `W_shared[o * ws_sq + j]` - the same address! This is a broadcast, which is efficient in shared memory. And `in_shared[j * channels_per_head + c]` with c = threadIdx.x: threads access different addresses (different banks), so no bank conflicts. Excellent! One more thing: I should consider using `float` accumulation to avoid precision loss. The matmul is 49 terms of multiply-add, so accumulation error should be minimal. Now, the output writing: ```cuda int idx = ((b * H + y_orig) * W + x_orig) * C + h * channels_per_head + c; output[idx] = sum; ``` For a fixed (b, y_orig, x_orig, h, o), and c = threadIdx.x varying: `idx` varies by 1 for consecutive threads. So writes are coalesced! This is perfect. Now let me think about launch configuration. I want to process all heads in parallel, so using 2D grid with y-dimension for heads is good. The Conv2d is well-optimized. `PatchMerging`: ```python x0 = x[:, 0::2, 0::2, :] x1 = x[:, 1::2, 0::2, :] x2 = x[:, 0::2, 1::2, :] x3 = x[:, 1::2, 1::2, :] x = torch.cat([x0, x1, x2, x3], -1) x = x.view(B, -1, 4 * C) x = self.norm(x) x = self.reduction(x) ``` The slicing `0::2` and `1::2` creates non-contiguous views. The `cat` along the last dimension concatenates C-sized chunks. This could be done more efficiently with a single `reshape` + `permute`. Actually, the standard way to implement PatchMerging more efficiently is: ```python B, H, W, C = x.shape x = x.reshape(B, H//2, 2, W//2, 2, C).permute(0, 1, 3, 4, 2, 5).reshape(B, -1, 4*C) ``` Wait, let me verify: Original: x is (B, H, W, C) reshape(B, H//2, 2, W//2, 2, C) permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5) Wait, the standard patch merging in Swin actually does: x0 = x[:, 0::2, 0::2, :] -> positions (even, even) x1 = x[:, 1::2, 0::2, :] -> positions (odd, even) x2 = x[:, 0::2, 1::2, :] -> positions (even, odd) x3 = x[:, 1::2, 1::2, :] -> positions (odd, odd) Then cat along C: (B, H/2, W/2, 4*C) Using reshape and permute: ```python x = x.reshape(B, H//2, 2, W//2, 2, C) x = x.permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5).reshape(B, H//2, W//2, 4*C) ``` Wait, permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5) on (B, H//2, 2, W//2, 2, C): - dim 0: B - dim 1: H//2 - dim 2: W//2 - dim 3: 2 (from original dim 2) - dim 4: 2 (from original dim 4) - dim 5: C Shape: (B, H//2, W//2, 2, 2, C) Reshape to (B, H//2, W//2, 4*C) Is this the same as cat([x0, x1, x2, x3], -1)? In the permuted tensor, for a fixed (b, h, w), the elements are: [h_index, 0, w_index, 0, c], [h_index, 0, w_index, 1, c], [h_index, 1, w_index, 0, c], [h_index, 1, w_index, 1, c], for all c. Wait, no. The dims are (B, H//2, W//2, 2, 2, C). The two 2s come from: - dim 3 = original dim 2: the h-parity (0=even, 1=odd) - dim 4 = original dim 4: the w-parity (0=even, 1=odd) So for fixed (b, h, w), elements are: [h_parity, w_parity, c] for h_parity in {0,1}, w_parity in {0,1}, c in {0..C-1} When reshaped to (B, H//2, W//2, 4*C), the order is: - First C elements: h_parity=0, w_parity=0 -> x0 - Next C elements: h_parity=0, w_parity=1 -> x2 - Next C elements: h_parity=1, w_parity=0 -> x1 - Next C elements: h_parity=1, w_parity=1 -> x3 Wait, that's not the same order as `cat([x0, x1, x2, x3], -1)`! The reference concatenates x0, x1, x2, x3, but the reshape gives x0, x2, x1, x3. Does the order matter? Only if there's a norm or linear layer afterwards that expects a specific order. The reference uses: ```python x = torch.cat([x0, x1, x2, x3], -1) x = x.view(B, -1, 4 * C) x = self.norm(x) x = self.reduction(x) ``` `self.norm` is LayerNorm on the last dimension (4*C), which normalizes each channel independently. The order of channels doesn't matter for LayerNorm. `self.reduction` is a Linear layer from 4*C to 2*C. The Linear layer has a weight matrix of shape (2*C, 4*C). If we permute the input channels, the output changes! So the order DOES matter for the Linear layer. We can't arbitrarily permute the channels without adjusting the Linear weight matrix. But actually, if we reshape differently to match the exact order, we could avoid the 4 separate slicing operations and the cat. However, the reshape+permute approach would require rearranging the reduction weight matrix accordingly. This is a complex optimization. I'll skip it for now. Back to the main plan. OK, drafting the code now: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline import types # Load the custom CUDA kernel cuda_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void swin_spatial_mlp_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int H, int W, int C, int num_heads, int ws, int shift_size ) { int _H = H + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0); int _W = W + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0); int nWH = _H / ws; int nWW = _W / ws; int window_idx = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; if (window_idx >= B * nWH * nWW || h >= num_heads) return; int b = window_idx / (nWH * nWW); int rem = window_idx % (nWH * nWW); int wy = rem / nWW; int wx = rem % nWW; int P_t = (shift_size > 0) ? (ws - shift_size) : 0; int P_l = (shift_size > 0) ? (ws - shift_size) : 0; int channels_per_head = C / num_heads; int ws_sq = ws * ws; extern __shared__ float shared_mem[]; float* W_shared = shared_mem; float* in_shared = shared_mem + ws_sq * ws_sq; const float* W_ptr = weight + h * ws_sq * ws_sq; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < ws_sq * ws_sq; i += blockDim.x) { W_shared[i] = W_ptr[i]; } for (int j = 0; j < ws_sq; j++) { int jy = j / ws; int jx = j % ws; int y_padded = wy * ws + jy; int x_padded = wx * ws + jx; int y_orig = y_padded - P_t; int x_orig = x_padded - P_l; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < channels_per_head; c += blockDim.x) { float val = 0.0f; if (y_orig >= 0 && y_orig < H && x_orig >= 0 && x_orig < W) { int idx = ((b * H + y_orig) * W + x_orig) * C + h * channels_per_head + c; val = input[idx]; } in_shared[j * channels_per_head + c] = val; } } __syncthreads(); for (int o = 0; o < ws_sq; o++) { for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < channels_per_head; c += blockDim.x) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < ws_sq; j++) { sum += W_shared[o * ws_sq + j] * in_shared[j * channels_per_head + c]; } if (bias != nullptr) { sum += bias[h * ws_sq + o]; } int oy = o / ws; int ox = o % ws; int y_padded = wy * ws + oy; int x_padded = wx * ws + ox; int y_orig = y_padded - P_t; int x_orig = x_padded - P_l; if (y_orig >= 0 && y_orig < H && x_orig >= 0 && x_orig < W) { int idx = ((b * H + y_orig) * W + x_orig) * C + h * channels_per_head + c; output[idx] = sum; } } } } torch::Tensor swin_spatial_mlp_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, int shift_size, int window_size ) { TORCH_CHECK(input.is_cuda(), "input must be a CUDA tensor"); TORCH_CHECK(input.is_contiguous(), "input must be contiguous"); TORCH_CHECK(input.dtype() == torch::kFloat32, "input must be float32"); TORCH_CHECK(weight.is_cuda(), "weight must be CUDA"); TORCH_CHECK(weight.dim() == 3, "weight must be 3D"); auto B = input.size(0); auto H = input.size(1); auto W = input.size(2); auto C = input.size(3); auto num_heads = weight.size(0); auto ws = window_size; auto ws_sq = ws * ws; TORCH_CHECK(weight.size(1) == ws_sq, "weight dim 1 mismatch"); TORCH_CHECK(weight.size(2) == ws_sq, "weight dim 2 mismatch"); TORCH_CHECK(C % num_heads == 0, "C must be divisible by num_heads"); int _H = H + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0); int _W = W + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0); int nWH = _H / ws; int nWW = _W / ws; auto output = torch::empty_like(input); int num_windows = B * nWH * nWW; dim3 grid(num_windows, num_heads); int channels_per_head = C / num_heads; int block_size = channels_per_head; if (block_size > 512) block_size = 512; if (block_size < 32) block_size = 32; int shared_mem_size = ws_sq * ws_sq * sizeof(float) + ws_sq * channels_per_head * sizeof(float); swin_spatial_mlp_kernel<<<grid, block_size, shared_mem_size>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), (bias.defined() && bias.numel() > 0) ? bias.data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, B, H, W, C, num_heads, ws, shift_size ); return output; } ''' swin_ext = load_inline( name="swin_spatial_mlp", cpp_sources="", cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["swin_spatial_mlp_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False, ) ``` Wait, do I need to include `cpp_sources`? So each group has `ws*ws` input channels and `ws*ws` output channels, and operates over `cph` spatial positions. In cuDNN/Conv1d, for each output channel in a group, and for each spatial position `l` (which is `cph` here), it computes: `y[b, g*ws^2 + o, l] = sum_{j=0}^{ws^2-1} W[g*ws^2 + o, j] * x[b, g*ws^2 + j, l] + bias[g*ws^2 + o]` Where `b` ranges from `0` to `nW*B - 1`, `g` ranges from `0` to `nH - 1`, `o` ranges from `0` to `ws^2 - 1`, and `l` ranges from `0` to `cph - 1`. In my current kernel, I have: - grid.x = window_idx (which is `b * nWH * nWW + wy * nWW + wx` for padded windows) - grid.y = h (head index `g`) - threadIdx.x iterates over `c` (which is `l`, the channel within a head, ranging from `0` to `cph - 1`) For each `(window_idx, h)` block: - Load `W[h, o, j]` for all `o, j` into shared mem. - For each `j`, load `input[b, y, x, h*cph + c]` where `y, x` depend on the window and `j`, into shared mem as `in_shared[j * cph + c]`. - For each `o` and `c`, compute `sum_j W[h, o, j] * in_shared[j * cph + c]` and add bias. - Write back to `output[b, y, x, h*cph + c]` where `y, x` depend on window and `o`. The computation is: `output[b, wy, wx, o, c] = sum_j W[h, o, j] * input[b, wy, wx, j, c] + bias[h, o]` This exactly matches the Conv1d formula where: - `b` is the combined window+batch index - `h` is the group index - `j` is the input spatial index within the window - `o` is the output spatial index within the window - `c` is the "length" dimension of Conv1d (which is `cph`) Wait, in my kernel, `nW*B` is actually `B * nWH * nWW` (the padded number of windows), not `nW*B` from the reference. For `shift_size=0`, these are the same. For `shift_size > 0`, the reference's `window_partition` on padded tensor gives exactly `B * _H/ws * _W/ws = B * nWH * nWW`. So `nW` in the reference is `nWH * nWW`. So `nW*B` = `B * nWH * nWW`. This matches my `window_idx` range. Good. So the mathematical formula is identical. The only difference is accumulation order. Now, in cuDNN's Conv1d, for `kernel_size=1`, it's essentially a batched GEMM. The accumulation is done via cuBLAS, which uses highly tiled and parallelized algorithms (e.g., splitting the K dimension across threads). My kernel, with `block_size = cph` (or up to 512), assigns each thread one `c` and has it accumulate `ws_sq` terms sequentially. The parallelization is over `c` and over `(window, head)`. For small matrices like `(ws_sq, ws_sq) = (49, 49)`, cuBLAS might use a specific algorithm. The order of summing the 49 terms differs. How can I make the accumulation order more deterministic or match cuBLAS? One option is to accumulate in chunks. But without knowing cuBLAS's specific algorithm, it's impossible to match exactly. However, the difference is very small (2e-4 max). Maybe I can pass if I use slightly higher precision in shared memory or accumulate in a specific way. But a single sequential sum is actually one of the most accurate ways for 49 terms. Actually, for 49 terms of random values in [-1, 1], the expected rounding error from sequential summation is about `sqrt(49) * eps ~ 7 * 1e-7 = 7e-7`. Here `stride[1] = C` and `stride[2] = 1`, which is contiguous for `(C_in, L)`. So cuDNN can process this directly. Now, what does the Conv1d do with groups=nH? This is valid because `nH * ws*ss*cph = ws*ss*C`. Yes, it's a view. Then `reshape(-1, ws, ws, C)` -> stride `(ws*ss*C, ws*C, C, 1)`. For the input at `(b, y, x, c)`, with `c = h*cph + l`, the element corresponds to: `n = b*nWH*nWW + wy*nWW + wx` (for shift_size=0) `j = (y % ws) * ws + (x % ws)` And in the layout for Conv1d: `x_conv[n, h*ws*ss + j, l] = input[b, y, x, h*cph + l]` My kernel reads `input[((b*H + y)*W + x)*C + h*cph + l]`. Then `.contiguous()` copies the data to a new memory with row-major strides corresponding to the new logical shape. For `permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5)`, the new logical shape is `(B, nWH, nWW, ws, ws, C)`. The permuted tensor's strides map logical `(b, wy, wx, jy, jx, c)` to physical address. For the logical window partition element `w = b, wy, wx, jy, jx, c`, the physical address in the original tensor is: `b*H*W*C + wy*ws*W*C + jy*W*C + wx*ws*C + jx*C + c` (Derived from strides: stride_of_dim0 * b + stride_of_dim1 * wy + ...) Correct. `stride_of_dim3 = W*C` in permute. `jy*W*C`. Correct. `stride_of_dim2 = ws*C` in permute. `wx*ws*C`. Correct. `stride_of_dim4 = C` in permute. `jx*C`. Correct. So yes, the physical address is correct. And the `.contiguous()` copy stores it at the new row-major index. Now, after `view(-1, ws*ws, C)`, the stride becomes `(ws*ss*C, C, 1)`. The windows are stored contiguously in memory, but within a window, the elements are stored in row-major (y, x) order. In the original tensor, moving in x direction increments by C, but moving in y direction increments by W*C. In `window_partition`, the permute is `(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5)`, which places `ws` (dim2) before `ws` (dim4). Let's re-examine the strides. After `view(B, H//ws, ws, W//ws, ws, C)`: - dim0 (B): stride = product of all other dims = H*W*C - dim1 (H//ws): stride = ws*W*C - dim2 (ws): stride = W*C - dim3 (W//ws): stride = ws*C - dim4 (ws): stride = C - dim5 (C): stride = 1 After `permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5)`: - new dim0 (B): old dim0 -> stride = H*W*C - new dim1 (H//ws): old dim1 -> stride = ws*W*C - new dim2 (W//ws): old dim3 -> stride = ws*C - new dim3 (ws): old dim2 -> stride = W*C - new dim4 (ws): old dim4 -> stride = C - new dim5 (C): old dim5 -> stride = 1 So for a logical element `(b, wy, wx, jy, jx, c)` in the permuted tensor, the memory address is: `base + b*H*W*C + wy*ws*W*C + wx*ws*C + jy*W*C + jx*C + c` And we want this to equal the original address of `(b, y=wy*ws+jy, x=wx*ws+jx, c)`: `base + b*H*W*C + y*W*C + x*C + c` `= base + b*H*W*C + (wy*ws+jy)*W*C + (wx*ws+jx)*C + c` `= base + b*H*W*C + wy*ws*W*C + jy*W*C + wx*ws*C + jx*C + c` But from permute strides: `base + b*H*W*C + wy*ws*W*C + wx*ws*C + jy*W*C + jx*C + c`. Since addition is commutative, these are the same! `wx*ws*C + jy*W*C` vs `jy*W*C + wx*ws*C`. Yes, same thing. Good. A common cause of numerical mismatch in custom CUDA kernels is the use of FMA (fused multiply-add) instructions. In CUDA, the compiler might generate `fmaf` instructions, which do `a*b+c` with a single rounding instead of two. PyTorch's Conv1d (via cuBLAS) also uses FMA heavily, but the order of FMAs might differ. However, the difference should still be small. Another idea: what if the issue is that the custom kernel does not handle the case where `block_size < channels_per_head` correctly in terms of __syncthreads? Let me check. In my kernel: ```cpp for (int j = 0; j < ws_sq; j++) { for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < channels_per_head; c += blockDim.x) { ... in_shared[j * channels_per_head + c] = val; } } __syncthreads(); for (int o = 0; o < ws_sq; o++) { for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < channels_per_head; c += blockDim.x) { ... } } ``` When `block_size < channels_per_head`, some threads write multiple elements across the `j` loop into `in_shared`. The `__syncthreads()` ensures all writes are visible before reads. This is correct. But wait: `block_size` is set to `channels_per_head` unless `channels_per_head > 512`, in which case `block_size = 512`. For early layers, `C` is small. Could the issue be bank conflicts in shared memory? For `in_shared[j * 32 + c]`, when threads read/write with consecutive `c` (threadIdx.x = 0..31), they access consecutive 32-bit floats. In shared memory, consecutive addresses map to consecutive banks (32 banks). So 32 threads access 32 different banks - no bank conflicts. Good. For `W_shared[o * 49 + j]`, all threads in a warp read the same `W_shared[...]` element simultaneously. This causes a broadcast, which is handled efficiently in shared memory (no bank conflict for broadcast). So shared memory access is optimal. Wait, I just thought of something: the `torch::empty_like(input)` for the output is uninitialized. But I always write to every valid output element. The view reads all elements in row-major order. For a tensor of shape `(B, H, W, C)`, `view(B, H*W, C)` just changes metadata. It doesn't touch memory. So even if there are garbage values outside the written region... wait, I write to ALL elements in the `(B, H, W, C)` tensor. For each `(b, y, x, c)`, there is exactly one `(window, head, o)` that writes to it. Let me verify this. For `shift_size = 0`, each element `(b, y, x, c)` belongs to exactly one window: `wy = y / ws`, `wx = x / ws`, `o = (y % ws) * ws + (x % ws)`. So exactly one block writes to it. For `shift_size > 0`, each element `(b, y, x, c)` in the original image is covered by exactly one padded window: the padded coordinates are `y_padded = y + P_t`, `x_padded = x + P_l`. Then `wy = y_padded / ws`, `wx = x_padded / ws`, `o = (y_padded % ws) * ws + (x_padded % ws)`. Since `P_t = ws - shift`, and `0 <= y < H`, we have `P_t <= y_padded < H + P_t < H + ws = _H`. And `_H` is divisible by `ws`. So `y_padded` falls within `[P_t, _H - P_b)` (since `H + P_t = _H - P_b`). Wait, `y_padded < H + P_t = H + ws - shift`. And `_H - P_b = H + ws - shift`. So `y_padded` is in `[ws-shift, H+ws-shift)`, which is `[P_t, _H - P_b)`. And `wy = y_padded / ws`. So `wy` ranges from `0` to `nWH - 1`. For `y_padded < _H - P_b`, we have `wy*ws + oy = y_padded`, and `oy < ws`. Since `y_padded >= P_t`, `oy` could be such that `y_padded - P_t = y >= 0`. The kernel checks `y_orig >= 0` and `y_orig < H` before writing. So for valid elements, there is exactly one writer. What about padded positions outside the original image (e.g., corners padded by shift)? Those have `y_orig < 0` or `y_orig >= H` or `x_orig < 0` or `x_orig >= W`. My kernel does NOT write to those positions. But in the reference, after `window_reverse`, those padded positions ARE filled (with computed values from zero-padded windows), and then cropped out. So in my output tensor, those "padded" positions contain whatever garbage was in `torch::empty_like`. But I never read them! Because I `view(B, H*W, C)`, which reads the entire tensor in row-major order. There are no padded corners in my output tensor. My output tensor has shape `(B, H, W, C)`. In the reference, after cropping, the output also has shape `(B, H, W, C)`. The reference's `shifted_x` is `(B, _H, _W, C)`, and after crop it becomes `(B, H, W, C)`. My kernel directly produces `(B, H, W, C)`. For positions within `[0, H) x [0, W)`, both should be correct. There are exactly `B*H*W*C` such positions, and each has exactly one writer. So ALL elements in the output tensor are initialized by my kernel. There are no uninitialized elements. Good. Let me test in Python: ```python import torch N = 2; ws = 7; nH = 3; cph = 32; C = nH * cph # 96 x = torch.randn(N, ws, ws, C) # contiguous x = x.view(N, ws*ws, C) y = x.view(N, ws*ss, nH, cph) z = y.transpose(1, 2) w = z.reshape(N, nH*ws*ss, cph) print(w.is_contiguous()) ``` I can't run Python here, but from my earlier analysis, the strides of z are `(ws*ss*C, cph, C, 1)`. For w with shape `(N, nH*ws*ss, cph)`, the expected contiguous stride is `(nH*ws*ss*cph, cph, 1)`. Our stride would be `(ws*ss*C, C, 1) = (ws*ss*nH*cph, nH*cph, 1)`. Since `stride[1] = nH*cph != cph`, w is not contiguous. And the strides are not compatible for a view because the second dimension's stride should be `cph` for a contiguous tensor, but it's `nH*cph`. However, PyTorch's `reshape` might still create a view with non-standard strides if the total memory layout allows it. I believe PyTorch's `reshape` will prefer to return a view if possible. Is this stride layout valid for a view? Yes, because `stride[1] == size[2] * stride[2] * something`? Actually, no: for a view, there is no constraint other than that the strides are consistent with the underlying storage. The underlying storage is contiguous with shape `(N, ws*ss, C)` and stride `(ws*ss*C, C, 1)`. The view `(N, nH*ws*ss, cph)` accesses elements in this storage. Is every element accessed exactly once? Let's check: the total number of elements is `N * nH*ws*ss * cph = N*C*ws*ss`. The underlying storage has `N*ws*ss*C` elements. They match. The mapping: index `i` in the view corresponds to storage element: `storage_idx = i[0]*stride[0] + i[1]*stride[1] + i[2]*stride[2]` `= n*ws*ss*C + (h*ws*ss + j)*nH*cph + c` `= n*ws*ss*C + h*ws*ss*nH*cph + j*nH*cph + c` `= n*ws*ss*C + h*ws*ss*C + j*C + c` `= (n*ws*ss + h*ws*ss + j)*C + c` In the original contiguous storage `(N, ws*ss, nH, cph)` (which is what `x_windows.view(N, ws*ss, nH, cph)` has), the element at `(n, j, h, c)` is at: `storage_idx = n*ws*ss*C + j*C + h*cph + c` `= (n*ws*ss + j)*C + h*cph + c` These are DIFFERENT! In the view `(N, nH*ws*ss, cph)`, element `(n, h*ws*ss + j, c)` maps to `(n*ws*ss + h*ws*ss + j)*C + c`. But in the original storage, the element at the corresponding logical position is `(n*ws*ss + j)*C + h*cph + c`. So the view is accessing DIFFERENT physical elements than the intended logical mapping! Wait, that means `reshape` after `transpose` MUST be making a copy, because the transposed tensor's memory layout cannot be reshaped to the desired shape without reordering! Let me verify: After transpose, the tensor has shape `(N, nH, ws*ss, cph)` and stride `(ws*ss*C, cph, C, 1)`. The underlying storage is a contiguous block of size `N*C*ws*ss`. The element at `(n, h, j, c)` is at memory offset: `n*ws*ss*C + h*cph + j*C + c`. If we reshape to `(N, nH*ws*ss, cph)`, the element at `(n, h*ws*ss + j, c)` should ideally be at the same memory offset as the original element `(n, h, j, c)`. Memory offset for `(n, h*ws*ss + j, c)` in a contiguous tensor would be: `n*C*ws*ss + (h*ws*ss + j)*cph + c`. But in the transposed tensor, it's: `n*C*ws*ss + h*cph + j*C + c`. For these to be equal: `(h*ws*ss + j)*cph + c = h*cph + j*C + c` `h*ws*ss*cph + j*cph + c = h*cph + j*C + c` `h*C + j*cph = h*cph + j*C` `h*(C - cph) = j*(C - cph)` `(h - j)*(C - cph) = 0` This only holds if `h == j` or `C == cph` (i.e., `nH=1`). Since neither is generally true, the reshape CANNOT be a view; it MUST be a copy (or a non-contiguous reshape with non-standard strides). Wait, but earlier I showed that `reshape` returns something with strides `(ws*ss*C, C, 1)` and shape `(N, nH*ws*ss, cph)`. Is this valid? Let's check if any two indices map to the same storage offset. The mapping is: `f(n, k, c) = n*ws*ss*C + k*C + c` where `k = h*ws*ss + j`. Wait, with stride `(ws*ss*C, C, 1)`, the offset is indeed `n*ws*ss*C + k*C + c`. But the actual data in the storage at that offset came from the original logical position `(n, y, x, c')` where the original tensor's stride produces that offset. In the transposed tensor, the element at logical `(n, h, j, c)` is at offset `g(n, h, j, c) = n*ws*ss*C + h*cph + j*C + c`. The `reshape` to `(N, nH*ws*ss, cph)` creates a new logical indexing `k = h*ws*ss + j`. If we just keep the same strides, the offset for `(n, k, c)` is `n*ws*ss*C + k*C + c = n*ws*ss*C + (h*ws*ss + j)*C + c = n*ws*ss*C + h*C*ws*ss + j*C + c`. But this is NOT equal to `g(n, h, j, c) = n*ws*ss*C + h*cph + j*C + c` unless `h*C*ws*ss = h*cph`, which is false. So if `reshape` were to return a view with the same strides, the element at `(n, k, c)` would point to a completely wrong memory location. PyTorch's `reshape` would detect that the transposed tensor's memory layout is incompatible with the new shape and would make a COPY instead. Yes! This means `x_windows_heads = x_windows_heads.reshape(...)` causes a memory copy! The intermediate tensor `(N, nH*ws*ss, cph)` is contiguous, which is what Conv1d needs. Similarly, after Conv1d: ```python spatial_mlp_windows = spatial_mlp_windows.view(-1, self.num_heads, self.window_size * self.window_size, C // self.num_heads).transpose(1, 2) ``` `view` on contiguous output -> valid view. `transpose(1, 2)` -> non-contiguous. Then `reshape(-1, self.window_size * self.window_size, C)` -> must be a copy because the transpose breaks contiguity for the reshape. Wait, let's check. After `view(-1, nH, ws*ss, cph)` on contiguous tensor, strides are `(nH*ws*ss*cph, ws*ss*cph, cph, 1)`. After `transpose(1, 2)`, strides become `(nH*ws*ss*cph, cph, ws*ss*cph, 1)`. Then `reshape(-1, ws*ss, C)`: we want to flatten `nH` and `ws*ss`. Current strides: dim0=`nH*ws*ss*cph`, dim1=`cph`, dim2=`ws*ss*cph`, dim3=`1`. Flattening dim1 and dim2 into `ws*ss`: offset for `(n, h, j, c)` is `n*nH*ws*ss*cph + h*cph + j*ws*ss*cph + c`. For reshape `(N, ws*ss, C)`, offset for `(n, j, c)` should be: for a view it would be `n*nH*ws*ss*cph + j*? + c`. There's no way to match. So this is also a copy. Then `reshape(-1, ws, ws, C)` from a contiguous `(N, ws*ss, C)` is a view. So in the reference, there are MULTIPLE copies and transposes: 1. `window_partition(...).contiguous()`: Copy 1 2. `reshape` after transpose: Copy 2 3. Conv1d output is naturally contiguous. 4. `view(...).transpose(1,2).reshape(...)`: Copy 3 (the reshape after transpose) 5. `window_reverse(...).contiguous()`: Copy 4 That's potentially 4 memory copies per block! If I could output this directly in contiguous form, that would be great. Let me design such a kernel. Kernel `swin_window_extract`: - Input: `(B, H, W, C)` - Output: `(B * nWH * nWW, nH * ws * ws, cph)` contiguous - Process: for each window `(b, wy, wx)`, and each head `h`, and each spatial `j`, and each channel `c`: `output[(b*nWH*nWW + wy*nWW + wx) * nH*ws*ss*cph + (h*ws*ss + j)*cph + c] = input[b, y, x, h*cph + c]` where `y, x` depend on padding and window. This is a simple gather/scatter kernel. After this, I can call `self.spatial_mlp(x_mlp)`, which operates on a perfectly contiguous tensor. The output of Conv1d is `(B*nWH*nWW, nH*ws*ss, cph)`. Then I write a scatter kernel `swin_window_scatter` that writes back to `(B, H, W, C)`. This avoids all intermediate copies! And since the Conv1d is the exact same PyTorch op, the result should be exactly numerically identical! Wait, this might not be faster than my fully custom kernel (because it involves two kernel launches + one Conv1d launch, rather than one fused kernel launch). But it avoids the 4 memory copies of the reference. On H100 with high memory bandwidth, memory copies are cheap but still take time. The savings from removing 4 copies per block + overhead of transposes might still yield a decent speedup. And the numerical correctness is guaranteed because the compute is identical. Let me think about how much work this saves. For a typical layer, `B=10`, `H'=W'=56` or smaller, `ws=7`, `C` up to 768. The intermediate tensor size is `B * nWH * nWW * ws*ss * C` = `B * H' * W' * C`, which is the same as the input size. So each copy moves `B*H'*W'*C` floats. For 12 blocks, total intermediate data copied could be significant. With my custom extract/scatter kernels, I only move this data once in each direction. The extract kernel reads `(B,H,W,C)` and writes the MLP input contiguously. The Conv1d reads/writes its input. The scatter kernel reads the MLP output and writes to `(B,H,W,C)`. In the reference: 1. `window_partition` copy: read(B,H,W,C) + write(N,ws,ws,C) 2. `reshape` after transpose: read(N,ws,ws,C) in non-contiguous pattern + write(N,nH*ws*ss,cph) 3. Let me implement this. Actually, I just thought of another optimization: what if the gather kernel also adds the bias? No, the Conv1d will do that. Let me write a simple gather kernel: ``` __global__ void swin_window_extract( const float* input, float* output, int B, int H, int W, int C, int num_heads, int ws, int shift_size ) { int _H = H + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0); int _W = W + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0); int nWH = _H / ws; int nWW = _W / ws; int ws_sq = ws * ws; int cph = C / num_heads; int n = blockIdx.x; // window index: 0..B*nWH*nWW-1 int h = blockIdx.y; // head index: 0..num_heads-1 int tid = threadIdx.x; // linear index within (ws_sq, cph) if (n >= B * nWH * nWW || h >= num_heads) return; int b = n / (nWH * nWW); int rem = n % (nWH * nWW); int wy = rem / nWW; int wx = rem % nWW; int P_t = (shift_size > 0) ? (ws - shift_size) : 0; int P_l = (shift_size > 0) ? (ws - shift_size) : 0; int total_inner = ws_sq * cph; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_inner; idx += blockDim.x) { int j = idx / cph; int c = idx % cph; int jy = j / ws; int jx = j % ws; int y_padded = wy * ws + jy; int x_padded = wx * ws + jx; int y_orig = y_padded - P_t; int x_orig = x_padded - P_l; float val = 0.0f; if (y_orig >= 0 && y_orig < H && x_orig >= 0 && x_orig < W) { int in_idx = ((b * H + y_orig) * W + x_orig) * C + h * cph + c; val = input[in_idx]; } int out_idx = ((n * num_heads + h) * ws_sq + j) * cph + c; output[out_idx] = val; } } ``` Wait, the output shape is `(B*nWH*nWW, num_heads, ws_sq, cph)`. But Conv1d expects `(N, C_in, L)` where `C_in = num_heads * ws_sq` and `L = cph`. So the output should be `(N, num_heads * ws_sq, cph)` contiguous. If I write `output[n * num_heads * ws_sq * cph + h * ws_sq * cph + j * cph + c]`, then each `n` block is contiguous with all heads. The shape `(N, num_heads*ws_sq, cph)` would have stride `(num_heads*ws_sq*cph, cph, 1)`. Wait, if I want `(N, nH*ws_sq, cph)`, then: `out_idx = n * nH * ws_sq * cph + (h * ws_sq + j) * cph + c` `= n * C * ws_sq + h * ws_sq * cph + j * cph + c` This is exactly the contiguous layout for `(N, nH*ws_sq, cph)`! Yes. Similarly, the scatter kernel reads from `(N, nH*ws_sq, cph)` and writes back to `(B, H, W, C)`: ``` int idx = ((n * num_heads + h) * ws_sq + o) * cph + c; // corresponds to output channel h*ws_sq + o, position c ``` For each `(n, h, o, c)`: `o_y = o / ws`, `o_x = o % ws` `y_padded = wy*ws + o_y`, `x_padded = wx*ws + o_x` `y_orig = y_padded - P_t`, `x_orig = x_padded - P_l` If valid, write to `output[((b*H + y_orig)*W + x_orig)*C + h*cph + c] = input[idx]`. This is very straightforward. Then in Python: ```python x = self.norm1(x) x = x.view(B, H, W, C) N = B * nWH * nWW x_mlp = swin_ext.swin_window_extract(x, shift_size, ws) # (N, nH*ws_sq, cph) contiguous spatial_mlp_out = self.spatial_mlp(x_mlp) # Conv1d, identical to reference x_out = swin_ext.swin_window_scatter(spatial_mlp_out, B, H, W, shift_size, ws) x = x_out.view(B, H * W, C) x = shortcut + x x = x + self.mlp(self.norm2(x)) ``` This should be numerically identical because `self.spatial_mlp` is the exact same Conv1d as the reference. So: - out_channels = num_heads * ws^2 - in_channels/groups = num_heads * ws^2 / num_heads = ws^2 - kernel_size = 1 Weight shape: (num_heads*ws^2, ws^2, 1) In the reference, the spatial_mlp receives input of shape (batch, channels, length) = (nW*B, num_heads*ws^2, C//num_heads). The Conv1d with kernel_size=1 is equivalent to a linear layer on the channel dimension (with broadcasting over the length dimension). For groups=num_heads, each group g maps input channels [g*ws^2 : (g+1)*ws^2] to output channels [g*ws^2 : (g+1)*ws^2]. The weight for group g is of shape (ws^2, ws^2, 1). After squeeze, it's (ws^2, ws^2). So `self.spatial_mlp.weight.squeeze(-1).view(self.num_heads, ws*ws, ws*ws)` is correct! Wait, could the issue be that group Conv1d actually transposes differently? Let me trace through more carefully. Conv1d weight layout: For group convolution, the weight is stored as all groups concatenated. For group g, the weight is at `weight[g*ws^2 : (g+1)*ws^2, :, :]`. Within each group, the weight matrix shape is (ws^2, ws^2, 1). In PyTorch Conv1d: - For output channel `g*ws^2 + o` (0 <= o < ws^2): `output[..., g*ws^2 + o, ...] = sum_j input[..., g*ws^2 + j, ...] * weight[g*ws^2 + o, j, 0] + bias[g*ws^2 + o]` So: - `W[g][o][j] = weight[g*ws^2 + o, j, 0]` In my code: ```python W_tensor = self.spatial_mlp.weight.squeeze(-1).view(self.num_heads, ws * ws, ws * ws) ``` So `W_tensor[g, o, j] = weight[g*ws^2 + o, j, 0]` That matches! This matches the F.pad in the reference. Then for the output in the reference: ``` spatial_mlp_windows = Conv1d(x_windows_heads).view(-1, num_heads, ws^2, C//num_heads).transpose(1, 2) # shape: (-1, ws^2, num_heads, C//num_heads) # spatial_mlp_windows[w, o, h, c] = result for window w, pos o, head h, chan c spatial_mlp_windows = spatial_mlp_windows.reshape(-1, ws^2, C) # shape: (-1, ws^2, C) # spatial_mlp_windows[w, o, :] has shape C # spatial_mlp_windows[w, o, h*cph+c] = result spatial_mlp_windows = spatial_mlp_windows.reshape(-1, ws, ws, C) # spatial_mlp_windows[w, oy, ox, :] now corresponds to position (oy, ox) within window w ``` Then window_reverse with _H, _W puts it back into spatial positions, and cropping removes padding. In my kernel output writing: ``` output[b, y_orig, x_orig, h*cph+c] = result for window (wy, wx), pos (oy, ox) ``` Where: ``` y_padded = wy*ws + oy x_padded = wx*ws + ox y_orig = y_padded - P_t x_orig = x_padded - P_l ``` This corresponds to: ``` spatial_pos = (wy, wx) window at local position (oy, ox) in padded coords = (y_padded, x_padded) in original coords = (y_orig, x_orig) ``` Which matches the reference perfectly. Different accumulation precision (e.g., PyTorch uses fp32 accumulation with different order) On H100 with cuDNN, Conv1d might use TF32 tensor cores. The TF32 format has 10 bits of mantissa vs 23 for fp32, giving about 3 decimal digits of precision vs 7. The tolerance is 1e-4, and TF32 might cause differences around 1e-4. Actually, I just realized: pyTorch's `nn.Conv1d` on a tensor of shape (batch, in_channels, length) with groups... this is just a batched matrix multiplication where the batch dimension is the sample index. For each sample n and each length position c: ``` output[n, g*ws^2+o, c] = sum_j input[n, g*ws^2+j, c] * W[g, o, j] + bias[g*ws^2+o] ``` This is exactly what my kernel does. The difference must be in the accumulation precision somewhere. Wait! Let me reread my kernel VERY carefully: ```cpp for (int o = 0; o < ws_sq; o++) { for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < channels_per_head; c += blockDim.x) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < ws_sq; j++) { sum += W_shared[o * ws_sq + j] * in_shared[j * channels_per_head + c]; } ``` Each thread processes one output channel `c` for one output position `o`. Since different threads process different `c`, this is fine. But all threads in the same warp are executing the same `o` loop, just with different `c`. The Swin MLP model has: - 2 + 2 + 6 + 2 = 12 SwinMLPBlocks - 3 PatchMerging layers The MLP in each block does: Linear(dim->4*dim) + GELU + Linear(4*dim->dim). The spatial_mlp does a Conv1d on (ws^2, ws^2) for each head. Given that the MLP has 4x the hidden dim and two linear layers, its computation cost is ~2 * dim * 4*dim * seq_len = 8*dim^2*seq_len FMAs. The spatial_mlp has cost ~num_windows * num_heads * ws^4 * cph = seq_len * ws^2 * dim FMAs. With ws=7, ws^2=49. For dim=96, 8*96^2 = 73728 vs 49*96 = 4704. So the MLP dominates at small dimensions. For dim=768, 8*768^2 = 4.7M vs 49*768 = 37.6k. The MLP is much more compute-heavy! So maybe optimizing the MLP would give bigger speedups. But the MLP is just two linear layers + GELU, which PyTorch already handles well. Wait, but the reference might be memory-bound due to all the reshape/transpose/contiguous ops. Let me count how many times data is copied in a SwinMLPBlock forward: 1. norm1: reads x, writes normalized (1 copy) 2. view(B, H, W, C): view (0 copy) 3. F.pad: writes padded (1 copy, only if shift_size > 0) 4. window_partition: contiguous (1 copy) 5. view: view (0 copy) 6. view + transpose: contiguous after transpose (1 copy) 7. reshape: view (0 copy) 8. Conv1d: writes output (1 copy) 9. view + transpose + reshape + reshape: views and contiguous (at least 1 copy) 10. window_reverse: contiguous (1 copy) 11. crop: slice + contiguous (1 copy, only if shift) 12. view: view (0 copy) 13. shortcut + residual: elementwise (1 read + 1 write, but in-place possible) 14. norm2 + MLP + residual: several copies Total: about 6-8 memory copies per block! That's a LOT of memory traffic. Fusing the window ops + spatial_mlp into one kernel could save 4-5 memory copies. That's where the speedup comes from! But I need to pass correctness first. Since my custom matmul seems correct (algorithmically) but has ~2e-4 error, maybe I should just use PyTorch's Conv1d inside my fused kernel. I can't call PyTorch from CUDA, but I can: 1. Extract windows to a temporary buffer using my CUDA kernel 2. Call PyTorch Conv1d on the buffer 3. Scatter back using my CUDA kernel This would save the intermediate reshape/transpose copies! And since the actual matmul is done by PyTorch, numerics would match perfectly. Let me design this: - Extract kernel: reads (B, H, W, C), writes (nW*B, num_heads, ws^2, cph) - contiguous - Call `self.spatial_mlp` on the reshaped tensor: input shape (nW*B, num_heads*ws^2, cph) - Scatter kernel: reads output of spatial_mlp, writes back to (B, H, W, C) Actually, I can pack extract differently: ```python # Extract windows as (B*nW, num_heads*ws^2, cph) # This is exactly what the reference does after all reshaping! ``` So my extract kernel would replace: `window_partition`, `view`, `view`, `transpose`, `reshape`. And my scatter kernel would replace: `view`, `transpose`, `reshape`, `reshape`, `window_reverse`, `crop`. And spatial_mlp stays as PyTorch Conv1d. This should save several memory copies and still match numerics perfectly! Let me implement this. The extract kernel: - Input: (B, H, W, C) - Output: (B*nWH*nWW, num_heads*ws^2, cph) contiguous - Each block handles one window, one head The scatter kernel: - Input: (B*nWH*nWW, num_heads*ws^2, cph) - Output: (B, H, W, C) - Each block handles one window, one head And then the custom forward becomes: ```python def custom_swin_forward(self, x): # ... norm1, view ... ws = self.window_size x_windows = swin_ext.swin_window_extract(x.contiguous(), self.shift_size, ws, self.num_heads) # x_windows shape: (nW*B, num_heads*ws^2, cph) x_out = self.spatial_mlp(x_windows) # Conv1d: (nW*B, num_heads*ws^2, cph) x = swin_ext.swin_window_scatter(x_out, B, H, W, self.shift_size, ws, self.num_heads) # ... residual, norm2, MLP ... ``` Wait, but x_windows from extract needs to have the exact same layout as what the reference produces before calling Conv1d. Let me verify. Reference: ```python x_windows = window_partition(shifted_x, self.window_size) # (nW*B, ws, ws, C) x_windows = x_windows.view(-1, self.window_size * self.window_size, C) # (nW*B, ws^2, C) x_windows_heads = x_windows.view(-1, ws^2, num_heads, cph) # (nW*B, ws^2, num_heads, cph) x_windows_heads = x_windows_heads.transpose(1, 2) # (nW*B, num_heads, ws^2, cph) x_windows_heads = x_windows_heads.reshape(-1, num_heads * ws^2, cph) # (nW*B, num_heads*ws^2, cph) ``` So x_windows_heads[n, h*ws^2 + j, c] = shifted_x[b, wy*ws + jy, wx*ws + jx, h*cph + c] Where n = b*nWH*nWW + wy*nWW + wx, j = jy*ws + jx. My extract kernel should write: ```cpp out[n * num_heads * ws_sq * cph + (h * ws_sq + j) * cph + c] = input[b, y, x, h*cph+c] ``` For spatial_mlp Conv1d output, shape is (nW*B, num_heads*ws^2, cph). Then x_out[n, h*ws^2+o, c] is the result. Scatter back: ```cpp output[b, y_orig, x_orig, h*cph+c] = x_out[n * num_heads * ws_sq * cph + (h * ws_sq + o) * cph + c] ``` Where y_orig = wy*ws + oy - P_t, x_orig = wx*ws + ox - P_l, o = oy*ws + ox. This is exactly matching the reference! And since the Conv1d is still PyTorch's, numerics will match. But wait, will this actually be faster? Let me think: - Original: pad(1 copy) -> partition(1 copy) -> view(0) -> view(0) -> transpose(1 copy) -> reshape(0) -> conv(1 copy) -> view(0) -> transpose(1 copy) -> reshape(0) -> reshape(0) -> reverse(1 copy) -> crop(1 copy) - My approach: extract(1 copy) -> conv(1 copy) -> scatter(1 copy) That's 7 copies in the original's spatial path vs 3 in mine. Plus I save the overhead of calling multiple Python/PyTorch ops. This should be significantly faster! However, there are a few concerns: 1. The extract/scatter kernels do non-contiguous memory access (strided loads/stores), which might be slow. 2. But global memory throughput should still be similar since the total bytes moved is the same. 3. The key savings are in fewer kernel launches and no intermediate buffers. Let me also think about whether I can fuse the norm1 into the extract kernel. norm1 is LayerNorm, which needs mean and variance over C channels for each spatial position. LayerNorm: `y = (x - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) * gamma + beta` If I fuse this into extract, I'd need to read C values, compute mean and var, normalize, then write to the window buffer. This reduces one memory pass! But LayerNorm uses learnable parameters from `self.norm1`, which I'd need to pass to the kernel. Actually, that's more complex. Let me start with just extract + conv + scatter. Wait, there's one more concern: the `x.contiguous()` call in my Python code. In the reference, after `norm1` on a tensor of shape (B, H*W, C), the result is contiguous. Then `view(B, H, W, C)` doesn't need a copy because it's contiguous. So `x` before the window ops IS contiguous. For shifted_x with padding: `F.pad(x, ...)` returns a contiguous tensor (it allocates new memory). So in my extract kernel, I can assume input is contiguous. Good. Let me implement this new approach. I need two kernels: 1. `swin_window_extract`: (B, H, W, C) -> (nW*B, num_heads*ws^2, cph) 2. `swin_window_scatter`: (nW*B, num_heads*ws^2, cph) -> (B, H, W, C) Both take shift_size, window_size, num_heads as parameters. I also need to handle the case where shift_size > 0 but min(input_resolution) <= window_size, in which case shift_size is set to 0. But this is already handled by the block constructor. Wait, actually in Model.__init__, window_size is always 7 and input_resolution starts at (56, 56), which is > 7. For later layers, it's (28, 28), (14, 14), (7, 7). For the last layer with (7, 7), min(resolution) = 7 = window_size. In the SwinMLPBlock constructor: ```python if min(self.input_resolution) <= self.window_size: self.shift_size = 0 self.window_size = min(self.input_resolution) ``` So for layer 4 with input_resolution=(7, 7), window_size becomes 7 (same), and shift_size becomes 0. So everything is fine - no shift in layer 4 because window covers the whole feature map. Wait, but in BasicLayer, the blocks are created with window_size=7 regardless of the layer. The SwinMLPBlock constructor adjusts it internally. So when my custom forward accesses `self.window_size`, it might be the ADJUSTED window_size (7), not the original. For layer 3 with input_resolution=(14, 14), window_size stays 7, shift_size alternates between 0 and 3. For layer 4 with (7, 7), window_size becomes 7, shift_size becomes 0 for all blocks. Alright, let me write the new kernels. I'll also handle the layer norm and residual in Python. One more thing: in the reference, after window operations and before the residual, there's `shortcut + self.drop_path(x)`. In the reference, `self.drop_path = nn.Identity()`, so it's just `shortcut + x`. In MLP: `x + self.drop_path(self.mlp(self.norm2(x)))`. Again, drop_path is Identity. So my custom forward can be: ```python def custom_swin_forward(self, x): H, W = self.input_resolution B, L, C = x.shape shortcut = x x = self.norm1(x) x = x.view(B, H, W, C) # Extract x_windows = swin_ext.swin_window_extract(x.contiguous(), self.shift_size, self.window_size, self.num_heads) # Spatial MLP via PyTorch Conv1d x_processed = self.spatial_mlp(x_windows) # Scatter back x = swin_ext.swin_window_scatter(x_processed, B, H, W, self.shift_size, self.window_size, self.num_heads) x = x.view(B, H * W, C) x = shortcut + x x = x + self.mlp(self.norm2(x)) return x ``` This replaces the entire window_partition -> spatial_mlp -> window_reverse path. Let me make sure `x_windows` has the right shape for the Conv1d: ```python # Extract kernel should output shape: (B*nWH*nWW, num_heads*ws^2, cph) # Because: # n = B * nWH * nWW # For each n, (num_heads * ws^2, cph) matrix ``` And `self.spatial_mlp` is `nn.Conv1d(num_heads*ws^2, num_heads*ws^2, kernel_size=1, groups=num_heads)`. Input to Conv1d: (batch, channels, length) = (n, num_heads*ws^2, cph) Output: (n, num_heads*ws^2, cph) Perfect! This matches exactly. Now, for the extract kernel, I need to know `cph = C / num_heads` inside the kernel. Since I pass `C` as input.size(3), I can compute it as `C / num_heads`. Likewise for scatter. Let me write the CUDA code: ```cpp __global__ void extract_kernel( const float* input, float* output, int B, int H, int W, int C, int num_heads, int ws, int shift_size ) { int _H = H + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0); int _W = W + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0); int nWH = _H / ws; int nWW = _W / ws; int ws_sq = ws * ws; int cph = C / num_heads; int n = blockIdx.x; // window index: B * nWH * nWW int h = blockIdx.y; // head index if (n >= B * nWH * nWW || h >= num_heads) return; int b = n / (nWH * nWW); int rem = n % (nWH * nWW); int wy = rem / nWW; int wx = rem % nWW; int P_t = (shift_size > 0) ? (ws - shift_size) : 0; int P_l = (shift_size > 0) ? (ws - shift_size) : 0; // output offset for this window and head float* out_ptr = output + (n * num_heads * ws_sq + h * ws_sq) * cph; for (int j = 0; j < ws_sq; j++) { int jy = j / ws; int jx = j % ws; int y_padded = wy * ws + jy; int x_padded = wx * ws + jx; int y_orig = y_padded - P_t; int x_orig = x_padded - P_l; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < cph; c += blockDim.x) { int idx = j * cph + c; float val = 0.0f; if (y_orig >= 0 && y_orig < H && x_orig >= 0 && x_orig < W) { int in_idx = ((b * H + y_orig) * W + x_orig) * C + h * cph + c; val = input[in_idx]; } out_ptr[idx] = val; } } } ``` Scatter kernel: ```cpp __global__ void scatter_kernel( const float* input, float* output, int B, int H, int W, int C, int num_heads, int ws, int shift_size ) { int _H = H + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0); int _W = W + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0); int nWH = _H / ws; int nWW = _W / ws; int ws_sq = ws * ws; int cph = C / num_heads; int n = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; if (n >= B * nWH * nWW || h >= num_heads) return; int b = n / (nWH * nWW); int rem = n % (nWH * nWW); int wy = rem / nWW; int wx = rem % nWW; int P_t = (shift_size > 0) ? (ws - shift_size) : 0; int P_l = (shift_size > 0) ? (ws - shift_size) : 0; const float* in_ptr = input + (n * num_heads * ws_sq + h * ws_sq) * cph; for (int o = 0; o < ws_sq; o++) { int oy = o / ws; int ox = o % ws; int y_padded = wy * ws + oy; int x_padded = wx * ws + ox; int y_orig = y_padded - P_t; int x_orig = x_padded - P_l; if (y_orig >= 0 && y_orig < H && x_orig >= 0 && x_orig < W) { int out_idx_base = ((b * H + y_orig) * W + x_orig) * C + h * cph; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < cph; c += blockDim.x) { output[out_idx_base + c] = in_ptr[o * cph + c]; } } } } ``` One critical thing: in `scatter_kernel`, when `y_orig` or `x_orig` is out of bounds (padding region), we should NOT write. And what about overlapping writes? For shift_size > 0, when a window's padding overlaps with the actual image, do different windows write to the same output location? Actually no! Each spatial position in the original H x W grid corresponds to exactly one window in the padded grid. Because: - For a pixel at (y, x) in the original image, in the padded grid it's at (y + P_t, x + P_l). - Window index for this pixel: wy = (y + P_t) / ws, wx = (x + P_l) / ws - Local position: oy = (y + P_t) % ws, ox = (x + P_l) % ws This maps uniquely to one window. So no overlapping writes. Good. For positions in the padded grid that are outside the original image, they're simply ignored (no write). This matches the reference behavior exactly. Let me also consider: in the reference, `window_reverse` returns a contiguous tensor `(B, _H, _W, C)`, then we slice `[:, P_t:-P_b, P_l:-P_r, :]`. This returns a contiguous slice. In my scatter approach, I directly write to the target positions in the output tensor. If the output tensor was zero-initialized, then positions outside the slice (which don't exist in the original image) remain zero. But in the reference, the shifted_x tensor returned by window_reverse doesn't have undefined values outside the crop region because `window_reverse` only writes from windows. Actually wait, `window_reverse` initializes the full `(B, _H, _W, C)` tensor and fills it from windows. Then cropping keeps only the valid region. In my scatter approach, if I write directly to the target positions and leave other positions as-is (from whatever the output tensor was before), that's fine as long as the output tensor is newly created and zero-initialized... or wait, in my custom forward, I want the output to be `shifted_x` but cropped. If I create the output tensor as `torch.empty(B, H, W, C)` and scatter into it, the uninitialized positions inside the HxW region that aren't written to could be garbage! But since every position in the HxW region IS written to exactly once, there's no issue. Let me verify: For every (y, x) in [0, H-1] x [0, W-1], in the padded grid: - y_padded = y + P_t - x_padded = x + P_l - wy = y_padded / ws, wx = x_padded / ws - oy = y_padded % ws, ox = x_padded % ws Since y_padded is in [P_t, H + P_t - 1] = [P_t, H + ws - shift_size - 1] And _H = H + ws (when shift_size > 0), so wy is in [0, _H/ws - 1]. And y_padded < H + P_t <= H + ws = _H. So wy < _H/ws. Similarly for wx. So every position is covered by exactly one window. No gaps, no overlaps. Wait, what if H is not a multiple of ws? The Swin architecture requires H and W to be multiples of ws. Actually, looking at the code, in BasicLayer, input_resolution is `(patches_resolution[0] // (2^i), patches_resolution[1] // (2^i))`. With patches_resolution computed from img_size/patch_size. For img_size=224, patch_size=4, patches_resolution=56. 56 is divisible by 7. When divided by 2: 28 (divisible by 7), 14 (divisible by 7), 7 (divisible by 7). So yes, always divisible. Every position is covered exactly once. Great, so my scatter approach is correct. One more optimization: I can initialize the output tensor with zeros using `torch.zeros` if needed, but since every position is written exactly once, `torch.empty` is fine and slightly faster. Now let me think about performance. The original does: 1. norm1 2. view 3. pad (contiguous copy when shift_size > 0) 4. window_partition (permute + contiguous) 5. view 6. view + transpose (permute + contiguous) 7. reshape 8. Conv1d 9. view + transpose (permute + contiguous) 10. reshape + reshape 11. window_reverse (permute + contiguous) 12. slice + contiguous My approach does: 1. norm1 2. view 3. extract kernel (moves data from BHWC to window format) 4. Conv1d 5. scatter kernel (moves data from window format back to BHWC) I've eliminated steps 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 9, 10, 11, 12, replacing them with steps 3 and 5 (two kernels instead of ~8 operations). This should be significantly faster! And since Conv1d is still PyTorch, numerics match exactly. Let me write the new code.
